RATING: 8
This is an extremely well designed game. It’s not particularly innovative in its approach – use buildings to turn resources into victory points - but the mechanics that are used in this approach are sufficiently different to other games of this type for it to provide a different challenge. There is also a level of competiveness / interaction in these mechanics that is higher than in many eurogames.

RATING: 7
Another amazing mashup of mechanics that I like, especially the using resources for both bidding and action/worker payment (with diminishing returns). Only complaint is that at 5/6p, everyone has to be super disciplined otherwise the game drags way more than it's worth. 2p isn't bad, but I'm more likely to either draft the starting pool of seasonal tiles, and/or use the Merchant expansion. 

So far, the promos have all been logical enough that you can live without them, or just throw them all in and forget about it. The exception to this would be the Key Celeste which has the ghost meeple element to scare off bids. The game doesn't suffer from a diminishing decision importance (ala Terra Mystica) in that you'll find pivot points for strategy that turn up in future seasons due to the draft (especially at player counts under 4). That doesn't detract from an overall strategic form of the game, it merely requires some risk management.

RATING: 9
A game of worker placement and bidding that lasts 4 rounds, scales from 2-6(!) players, is innovative despite using some familiar mechanics, and has high variability.

There are 4 seasons (rounds) in the game and each round a set number of tiles are laid out (this is where a large amount of the variability is introduced as there is a large number of different/unique tiles in the game), as well as a set number of boats carrying new workers and their skills into the new world.  A players turn is simple to execute but difficult to choose; you have the following options: 1) bid on a tile, 2) increase an existing bid on a tile, 3) utilize a tile, your own, another players or the ones currently up for bid, or 4) exchange workers.

The twist with the bidding and actions are that everything is colour specific; there are 4 colours in the game, and once a colour is used on a tile, only those workers of that colour can be used.  At the end of a round, winning bids collect their tiles and bring them into their own villages which involves a bit of tile-laying planning.  There are two main benefits of having your own tiles:1) any workers used on a tile are retained instead of lost at the end if around, and 2) the tiles are one of the main ways to score points (however they must be "flipped" in order to be worth points and this flipping also upgrades the tiles abilities.

The last game round is slightly different in that each player introduces their own tiles into the bidding pool and these tiles only function is for end game scoring.  

Gameplay is quick but thoughtful, there is a large amount of variety from game to game, it's works great at all player numbers, and the theme is decent.

Own the [boardgame=141816]emporium & monument[/boardgame], [boardgame=147380]storyteller[/boardgame], [boardgame=140936]Key Celeste[/boardgame], [boardgame=205504]Keymelequin[/boardgame], [boardgame=162647]beekeeper[/boardgame], [boardgame=180037]pig shelter[/boardgame] and [boardgame=163742]trader[/boardgame] expansion tiles, the [boardgame=144058]Farmers[/boardgame] expansion, and the [boardgame=163572]Merchants[/boardgame] expansion.

RATING: 8
My only previous experience with the Key games is from playing Keythedral. Keyflower is considerably heavier than that title. The rules-explanation is long, and the central concepts here are not particularly familiar from other titles, hence it isn't always intuitive. But Keyflower works brilliantly. It is clever, strategic and interactive, with just a touch of the mean-ness of Keythedral. The artwork is sublime, and the components are great. There is a lot of variety provided too, with different tiles available in different game, and variable scoring conditions. This is an excellent new take on the worker-placement, and pick-up-and-deliver genres.

RATING: 7.5
Keyflower is a game that sneaks up on you. It looks familiar with its hexes and meeples. It starts simple with just a small handful of familiar things to do. It has little familiar homes with little familiar chimneys and a nice familiar art style reminiscent of the coziest of childhood picture books. Then, when it gets your trust, it slaps you across the face.

Keyflower is Eddie Haskell. On the surface you're playing a little civilization game, building up an economy, putting your meeples to work, and upgrading your hexes into more beneficial properties. All the while you're whistling a tune and admiring the colorful and delightful artwork. But how did you get that property? You ruthlessly outbid somebody on it. Or you snuck in and snagged it at the last second with a color of meeple that was impossible to counter. Or maybe, just maybe, you drove up the bid on another property, only to back out and snag one that is of better use to you, just because you wanted your opponent to use up two more meeples than they needed to. You're doing it right, buddy. 

Not only that, but aren't you the one who is squatting on somebody else's land? You're the one using that one property that they purchased and that would help them this round, if only because you know that it will force them to use up more of their meeples. All the while, the lands are expanding, the hexes are becoming less easy to tame and the competition is heating up.

Don't let the aesthetic fool you. Keyflower may have a certain lighthearted charm to it, but beneath that veneer, there is a complex, escalating, and highly competitive soul.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Perhaps the game is better with 3 or more players because of its bidding mechanism, and after understanding how each tiles interacting with each other, and also what each season will offer. Probably because there's too little stuff going on compared to Bora Bora. The pick-up-and-deliver mechanism is a miss in our 2-players game as the tile needed to transport more resources is not in play. Our game finished in 30 minutes. The gameplay is very easy, it's either you bid for tiles or use any tiles or pass, with the requirement to use same colour meeples, increasing number of meeples used & no more than 6 meeples on a tile. The trick is to choose tiles that can give u the most points & work well with your other tiles.

It's very Euro-ish, one of the best game at any player count, random setup which means high replaybility, main mechanism is bidding, also has worker placement, tile placement, resource management, pick-up & deliver, network building, all the great Euro mechanics in 1 game! Best of Key series!

RATING: 8
6 plays / 3,4,5 players

LIKE: I love the town plus engine building. Elegant game design, tense auctions. Fun to figure out how to best use your army of meeples, either for bidding or as worker placement. The game ramps up in the last round, and can be a little cutthroat, in a fun way.

DISLIKE: Can be cutthroat at the end, players could block others just to slow them down if they have a good engine. This happens with the blacksmith, timber yard, and stone yard. Knowing tile combos gives slight advantage. 

OVERALL: Great game. On my first time through, getting to know the tiles and strategies was slow at first. In autumn and winter, the game became cutthroat as players started to block each other to prevent combo scoring. The second play was much more fun, because player see the value of the production tiles, and make sure to grab them.

RATING: 7
Very unique mechanics for the market, bidding and worker placement. Introduces fresh air into the genre. You are constantly thinking of trade-offs between this and that which is a sign of a good game. Personally I did not find the victory point generation mechanics that interesting. You are either upgrading tiles, moving resources onto certain tiles or collecting sets. Ok, this is basic stuff in board games but for some reason it felt a little bit detached in this context. Maybe it was the fact that acquiring tiles is not enough but you have to commit energy to make use of them. This slows the game down a little bit, especially on the first plays when you do not know what should you be aiming at. Moreover, it takes a lot of experience to see the value of each tile since you actually must be able to use them to unleash their potential.

I would call Keyflower a light gamer's game. There is no way could imagine teaching this to my non-gamer friends. The basic mechanics are so mind boggling at first that less experienced gamers will have hard time understanding the game. Yet they are rather simple once you have them figured out. It has to be mentioned though, that the rulebook plays its part in this initial complexity. The way the rules are written does a terrific job at explaining the rules but for some reason it is harder to understand how the game actually plays.

Keyflower definitely has potential for a higher rating than a seven. Time will tell.

RATING: 10
If I had invented a game, I wish it were this one. The game mechanic is relatively simple to teach quickly yet has enough depth to avoid "solving." Randomization in setup eliminates carbon-copy repeats of games, but it doesn't cancel planning (unlike See: Pirate's Cove). You have to plan ahead somewhat, but the inherent randomization in setup means you can't plan too far ahead (cuts down on analysis-paralysis; see: Agricola). You can almost always do something (if you have the workers for it), even if it costs you more (little blocking out; see: Agricola).
Basically, everything that I *don't* like about Agricola is fixed by Keyflower. :-)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This is apparently the 7th installment by R&D games for games in a setting known as "Key Land" which I like to think of as a theme park for locksmiths. "Unfamiliar to me" setting aside, there are a lot of interesting things going on in this game. Familiar mechanics are played in unfamiliar and novel ways. For instance, you spend the course of four seasons bidding on flops of tiles which you use to assemble your personal village. You don't bid with money though, you bid with meeples. The different colors must be matched and the quantity exceeded when bidding for a tile, so one blue meeple must be outbid by 2 blue meeples and so on. As you build your village, you want to use it to do things BUT other players can use it too so things can get tricky. Say for example you build a good engine, and another player sees it, well instead of trying to stop you they just decide to use your engine! Now this isn't a complete block, but in order to use a tile after someone else does, you again have to place extra meeples of the same color on the tile. What color meeples people have are secret so you may or may not be blocked when people do this to you and it is a part of the strategy in the game as well. These are just a few examples of mechanics that you thought you had seen done before executed differently. I really like this game and may have to check out other games in this, "Key Land".

RATING: 9
Keyflower is a game that has really grown on me recently. Many of the elements in the game are borrowed from other games, but the mix is original. Auction bidding, bluffing, tile-placement and even recource-managment are all parts of this brilliant design. When you first look at the game you will probably think that is not much depth to it because of the Carcasonne -feel with meeples and cute little city-tiles.

The decisions in this game are though, despite how the game look, very hard to make. I have a feeling this is a game that will stick. The more I play it, the more I like it.

RATING: 9
Played pre-release version. The familiar Breese traits of multiple options in this Key game are present, but in this game resource cubes are used to upgrade resource hextiles, rather than converted into different cubes. This change from other Key games makes Keyflower have a fresh feel. 

The games has 4 rounds each of which present a myriad of options, which means that games will be radically different. Player interaction is high as there is bidding for all hex tiles and you can use other player's tiles as well as your own. The number of choices you have is high and you need an initial game to get the hang of these. 

Early days yet but this might be the best Key game.

RATING: 8
1st play: very impressive game. For a long time I've wanted to try this and I'm glad I did. Great spatial city building elements combined with the tile flipping I like in Brass and oodles of meeples. Bonus point for the purple waving meeple. Being able to use another player's building but at the cost of giving them future workers is excellently implemented.

2nd play: The game was still good, the workers grow very smoothly, and I approached the game from a different angle this time. I didn't focus on worker acquisition, and instead aimed to flip as many tiles as possible. I was able to pull off a victory with a couple of high point tiles. My complaint with this game is that some end game tiles are worth 30 points, and some are worth 6, and that can create some huge swings in points. But it doesn't prevent me from liking the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
I've played this over 50 times online now, and I haven't grown the least bit tired of it yet. Can't wait to play my copy with expansions. I've played mostly two player, though also with three and four. I like the lower player counts the most. It has a strange feeling of being a very open game, while also being tense and cut-throat. Openness in that you can increase your bid for a tile, or even remove your bid and use the bidded workers somewhere else. You can even place your workers on a tile that is up for bid and use its action. You can use tiles in your own village for their action, or in another player's village. You can use a space that has already been used by a worker by simply using the same color of worker there, plus one (so if there is one yellow worker on the tile, you can take that action by putting two yellow workers on that tile, up to a maximum of three workers). Even the main final scoring tiles are up for auction and can be bid on by all players (each player gets a hand of three final scoring tiles at the start of the game, kept secret, and then must put at least one out for auction, or even all three). The game is cut-throat in that you can outbid a player for a tile they really need, or even block them from using their own tile in their village You maybe see that the worker batch your opponent took last round was almost entirely yellow and red workers, with one blue...you have several blue workers, so on your first move, you put two blue workers on that space, assuming he doesn't have three to use to activate it. Or, you can be very mean and just put three workers on a tile and completely block your opponent from using it. So while you are building your own village and taking actions that will let you upgrade your village tiles, maximize scoring opportunities, etc., you can also block your opponent from acquiring and/or using tiles. 

+Key Celeste
+Keymelequin
+Emporium and Monument
+The Farmers
+The Merchants

RATING: 7
A game that definitely rewards strategy and experience which is a big pro for me. On the other hand I believe that this will be difficult to bring other players in without having to 'go easy on them' for a few games.

A clear understanding of what tiles are available is absolutely necessary in order to score well and develop a strong strategy. Otherwise it's just shooting in the dark and a 'roll of the dice' whether you will score anything.

I love the light hearted art on top of a potentially ruthless game. I enjoy 'take that' and this is rife with it. Good use of risk/reward; do I spend my meeples to do this, or is it going to burn me because the other player has something up their sleeve?

We've only played a few times but I expect the more we play, the stronger the strategies and secondary strategies/tactics will become resulting in big surprises.

Not quite a 9 yet, but I could see it move up to it after a few more plays.

RATING: 7
I really like the mechanisms and how you're balancing several things at once as you portion out your meeples. I really like that the game gives you a wide and not too scripted range of paths to build and end up with a competitive score. On that basis, I want to give the game an 8 (a "great" game) since it's quite impressive how well it achieves those two things. But I can't. I can only give it 7 (a merely "good" game).

It has quite a few flaws that bring it down.

Firstly, the game is too short. You get four rounds to build an engine and then it ends. That's problematic because the first round your engine doesn't do anything, the second round it still doesn't do anything, the third round you realize that you still don't have a working motor, you're already halfway done, you don't know what tiles are going to come out, and you're really only going to have one chance to crank the motor and see what happens. The capabilities of the times would allow you to make something cool and multifaceted, but you're going to be stopped short and forced to go with a one trick pony. And that's all assuming that where you are at the end of the second round provides the foundation of a few possible engines and that, in the third and fourth rounds, tiles that are synergetic with what you've got going will come out, and that your opponents don't screw you up in some major way. There's really no room for error. While that makes it tense, it's a bunch of tenseness in aim of getting one successful crank on a one trick pony motor (woo), that you really were forced into based on the limitations of what showed up that was synergetic, and it makes it feel like the end game is a bit random. Were the tiles that you had to go for in rounds three and four a good high scoring combo or simply all you could do given the time constraints? High familiarity with the game might allow you to figure out how to start the engine so it leaves more high scoring options in place for later in the game (similar to knowing the best opening chess moves), but then you're just doing the prescribed motions for a full half of the game.

It's really half as long as it should be to have proper early, mid, and late games. But to achieve that, without turning it into an AP fest with anything more than 2 players, it would probably mean redesigning and rebalancing all of the tiles so that a larger map was feasible without raising the combinatoric potential of all action sequences too large. (Maybe implement "summoning sickness", raise the upgrade costs, and bump the transport distances?) As is, I'd probably suggest playing the game two player only and performing each season (except winter) twice, for the best experience.

My second issue with the game is that the iconography isn't terribly good. There's a basic hieroglyph system that it starts with that seems relatively straightforward. But already by season 2, that goes right out the door by introducing ongoing effects without any clear marker that this is the case. End game bonuses are introduced as a box within a box - except when they aren't. One of the meeple boats gives up and switches to English to tell us what the end game bonus is. It's fairly evident that the symbologist that developed the tiles decided at some point to give up in creating a language and, instead, simply created a "memory aid".

This is problematic, since it means that the person teaching the game has to spend with a bit of advance work talking about the special rules to do with the turn order tiles, the special rules with the meeple boats, explain some of the winter tiles, etc. And then during the game, at the start of each season, go over the powers of the tiles that were just set down.

The special rules would have been better dispensed with, given how flexible the basic engine of the game is. Just give us a set number of meeples each round, or build them into the tiles we add to our farms, or make the economy retain meeples rather than throwing them back into the bag each round. Don't build in the preset endings, just give us a longer game so that we can build our own engine.

Overall, the game is three times longer to teach than it needs to be, and the extra stuff doesn't benefit the game because all of the same things could have been accomplished with the systems that are already there or simpler systems.

And lastly, the issue is just theme. This effectively ties back into my issue with how few rounds the game has, but where I said before that there are mechanical issues with the shortness of the game, there's also the problem that you just don't feel like you're building a town. In my first game, I quickly decided that I wanted to be the "wood shaper", based on my early acquisitions. I was hoping that I would be able to get an early start in wood collection, move that up into crafting, sell my goods to other people, upgrade my wood crafters, and eventually start creating boats.

That's not there. You can make wood or you can make lots of wood. There's no real further progression on that path. Even with a longer game, a larger town, and all the combinatorics in the world, I would never have been able to do anything beyond amassing one of the simple types of resources.

In a game like Glen Moore, that doesn't bother me since nothing ever starts out looking like anything but an abstract Euro. And you have the combo action starting straight from turn 2 and lasting all of the way to the end. It makes up for its lack of story building by staying a small, simple, abstract game.

Keyflower feels too involved, the art looks too good, the farm looks too much like a farm, the meeples feel too much like workers and money (two real world, thematic things), and so I personally feel like I should be able to "build a farm".

With worse art and the ability to build a better engine that I could run a few times, maybe I'd overlook it, just as I do with Glen Moore, but that's not the game we have today.

Overall, I don't know that the game is significantly better or worse than Glen Moore or Glass Road - probably the two closest comparisons to Keyflower - but it feels like it could have been a lot better than both of those with a few small changes, and that seems like a loss worth complaining about.

RATING: 8

I liked the possibility of producing using other people's tiles and those available for auction this turn.  That and the first meeple placed on a tile setting the colour that others may use, bring some interesting possibilities for screwage. So Keyflower offers plenty of options and lots to consider.  I have played it three times now and it has been edging upwards in my estimation.  An efficiency game for sure, but one that brings a great deal of interaction to the genre. 

Production quality is good and the artwork is quite pleasing.  We found the rules rather wordy and vague on one or two points.

RATING: 10
Includes:
[thing=163572][/thing]

:arrowE: [u]Synopsis[/u]:
Keyflower is a great example of lots of mechanics going into a blender and managing to come out in perfect unity. You have worker placement, tile laying, dynamic auctions, set collection, hidden goals, variable turn order, pick up and deliver, resource conversion, and pretty boats. It's a remarkably fast-playing game for how much depth of interaction is present inside the box. The footprint is small, the upkeep is minimal, and the bluffing element of the (gorgeous) player screens heightens the tension as it encourages careful observation along with strategic and tactical play.

:arrowE: [u]What I like most[/u]:
Um, not to be a cop-out, but all of it. Seriously, so many familiar Eurogame trademarks are present in this game, but they gel together so well. For instance, in Spring, you're mostly just bidding on tiles, but then those tiles (and those from subsequent seasons) end up in your personal village and can be used without losing your meeples to the supply. Your engine begins to coalesce as you combo tiles together and begin to aim for end-game scoring. Other players use your village and give you their meeples, and vise versa. Action economy becomes paramount and color-blocking tiles becomes cutthroat and vicious. Winter tiles come out and it's a total knife fight, all with the humming of your personal scoring engine running full-tilt in the background.

:arrowE: [u]What I like least[/u]:
Depending on what tiles come out each season, there are (I think) a few strategies that can be overpowered in terms of action economy. Getting all of the meeple buildings and having a very imbalanced number of actions in comparison to other players, getting green meeples early to snipe critical tiles that you know other players are relying upon (especially in Winter!), and grabbing all available transportation + that one summer boat to use with the Keyflower after Winter all have potential for landslide victories. This is all to say that each session requires careful attention paid to the meta in order to keep everyone in check. This is both a strength and weakness, I think.

:arrowE: [u]Rating[/u]: [BGCOLOR=#00CC00] [b]10[/b] [/BGCOLOR]

RATING: 8
This is a well designed and interesting game, a fun and amusing variation on the standard worker placement mechanic.

You place your workers (which come in four separate bidding colors) on your designated bidding position on a euro-engine tile in the "shopping area" that you want to add to your tableau/village at the end of the round.  The number of workers you place down represents your bid.  If other players also want that euro-engine tile, they must place a greater number of their meeples, of the bidding color that you established with your first bid.  Any higher bids free up your workers to go somewhere else in that round.  Highest bidder wins the tile at the end of the round, and adds the euro engine tile to their village with some spatial considerations to take into account.  

Winning bidder meeples are "spent" and losing bidder meeples return to the player's supply for later use. At the beginning of each round, players sequentially and strategically select new boats of "arriving meeples," carrying randomly assigned meeple colors (for example 3 red meeples, 1 yellow, and 1 blue) to add to their supplies. 

Alternatively, rather than use your meeples to bid for a tile, you can use your workers to activate tiles, which provide euro effects, like obtaining resources, converting things to other things, transporting resources within your village, or flipping tiles which then provide VP's.  This is where the engine building can take place. 

And you can activate euro effect tiles in your village, in which case, very helpfully, these meeples are NOT spent, but come back to you in the next round.  Or, you can activate tiles in other people's villages, but at the end of the round, they will get those meeples! Or, very curiously, you can activate tiles that have not yet been won at the auction, in which case, these meeples go the player who wins the tile at the end of the round.  Wow, the permutations!

Thus, this game, when played seriously, can present mind blowing worker placement analysis issues, at least when I played with four.  The worker placement predictions, and analysis, can get VERY deep by the third (Fall) and fourth (Winter) rounds.

RATING: 9

The ability to use other players tiles helped bring a lot of interaction, and the auction phase made this one really stand out. I love engine building games and auction games, and Keyflower marries the genres beautifully.

RATING: 8
It's a fairly abstract point churner. It's also not a particularly forgiving game for new players; if you don't know where you are building your town, you'll do very poorly. The tiles (particularly the winter "6-cost" tiles) can be miserly in power they give out points. You can't just have stone for the stone building to count them; you have transport to you tile itself. Likewise, if a meeple is counted towards one tile, it doesn't count towards the other.

Still, the system is clever, such as in how the "engine-building" tiles become point generating tiles in the end. It goes surprisingly quick at lower player counts.

RATING: 9
only played 2-player so far.

My first play felt a little strange.  It felt very much that the game just abruptly ended just as it was really getting interesting.  Also (and this is to be expected I think) the bidding was very tame.

The second game was completely delightful.  The feel (in terms of weight and play time) reminds me of something like Glen More or Welcome to Walnut Grove.  All these games feel like a refinement of some of the heavier modern Euros (like Agricola).  With a better grip on the flow of the game and the value of the tiles there was lots to think about and the bidding more cut-throat.

There's a lot of really interesting decisions in the game especially in the timing of certain plays.  

With 2-players the play time is brisk (but I suspect that 3+ will introduce more down-time due to analysis)

Looking forward to playing again.

RATING: 8
[121028 - 5 játékossal]
A szabályt meghallgatva olyan semmilyennek tűnt...
Aztán a játék végére izzadt a tenyerem és éreztem, hogy kell a játék!

Egy év négy évszakján keresztül kell a kis településünket fejlesztgetni. Egy évszakban lapkákat szerzünk 3+1 fajta embereink kiküldése által, esetleg a saját, vagy az ellenfelek településein dolgoztatjuk őket a településünk felvirágoztatása érdekében.
Igen ötletes a licit rész (3+1 színű meeple által), Carcassonne-san jópofa a település a licit során megszerzett lapkák általi építése (út-úttal való csatlakozás). A nyersanyagszerzés elmaradhatatlan, továbbá a településünk "épületeinek" fejlesztése "logisztikai ismereteket" kíván.
Szóval az egész végtelenül egyszerű, de mégis komplex.

A játék címét megismerve kerestem a címben szereplő "virágot"...
Aztán rá kellett jönnöm, hogy talán azért "flower", mert a játék az idő előre haladtával úgy "nyílik ki" mint egy virág. 
A nyári és őszi évszakokban egyre több elérhető akció közül választhatunk (pl. Le Havre)

A játékidő abszolút elfogadható, a tartozékok minősége teljesen rendben van. Nekem tetszik a "KEY...." játékok stílusa. A tavalyi KeyMarket után ismét egy nagyszerű játék!
Szóval abszolút "Highly recommended"...!

RATING: 9
Great game. Six plays at Richmond BGG with 4 players. It's definitely a keeper, offering lots of difficult decisions and interaction - though it can lead to some AP and become overly slow. Took well over 2 hours to complete first game. Also, some combos seem very powerful - second game I got the Keywood and Sawmill next to each other and amassed 42 points from the Sawmill alone! However, with more plays, people become more aware of these possible combos and blocking them becomes much more likely.

RATING: 8.5
Quite original in the feeling.  Using your pawns, you can use tiles in your village and in other players village, use tiles from the table, or bid to get them to your village.  During three seasons you try to get the best tiles and more workers, and the final season you bid for scoring tiles.
Bidding and using tiles uses a color mechanism like the one in Asara.  If you use a tile with a blue pawn, the following players can only use it with two or threes blue pawns.  The incremental number has been used afterwards in Coal Barons.
All in all, quite intelligent and fun game.  Very nice design and production.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Possibly one of the best games of 2012!

Keyflower brings again to the table that distinctive "Key" feel through original mechanics and the usual great art from the Breese family, with an innovative game that's great even with 2 players.

The game lasts only four seasons, with each season bringing in the game a bunch of new village tiles. Players compete with bids to secure the preferred village tiles and add them to their village.
If this sounds not very innovative, one must consider that:
- there are meeples in three (plus one) different colors, but they're not associated with any player: they're simply different "nationalities" of immigrants, that "don't get along easily" with others
- on his turn, a player simply uses one or more of his meeples to place a bid on a tile (if won, it will be added to owner's village) OR activate a tile (own, of another player OR still up to bid!) for various benefits
- unlike many other worker placement games, once a tile is activated, it can be activated again by anyone simply by placing more workers OF THE SAME COLOR

The game is amazingly tight and full of meaningful decisions, even with just 2 players: for such a mid-weight and quite short game, that's amazing!

Initial tentative rating is an 8, but I can see it going up if it can stand repeated plays (and I don't really see why it wouldn't, given the potential for replayability due to the different mix of tiles dealt in each game...)!

RATING: 8
Initial Impression: Despite the presence of a horde of meeples, this euro game feels as if it breaks the "euro-mold". Sure, you place workers in order to activate tiles for their abilities. Yes, you collect resources to upgrade your tiles to increase efficiency and collect more VPs at the end of the game. However, placing multiple meeples in order to win tiles in an auction mechanism similar to Cyclades makes the game feel more interactive than many similar styled eurogames. The game moves at a brisk pace and has a staccato tempo as players quickly place meeples to outbid their opponents. Even the point salad ending doesn't feel laborious but rather helps to clearly define long term strategies for the players. I hope to play more of this game in the near future.

RATING: 8.5
Excellent auction game that might have one mechanism too many. The variety of tiles ensures each game plays differently. The tension of the auction and round-to-round decisions make for an exciting, engaging game that I will gladly play.

Update, Sept 2017:  The bidding with the various colored "Keyples" is great. The green color Keyple is great. The variation among the tiles is great. The ability to use your opponents' tiles is great. The movement puzzle, while adding to the game's variation (one of its strengths, no doubt) still feels, to me, a bit disjointed or superfluous. In essence, that feeling is rooted in the fact that so much of Keyflower is interactive, but the movement puzzle is not. It kind of breaks the cohesive feel to the game. Still, Keyflower is a great game if you want a contemplative, yet interactive, euro-style experience.

RATING: 9
A very intriguing game that starts harmless but quickly escalates into a very think-y and brain-burning game.
In the 3rd and 4th (of 4 rounds) you have a plethora of options but only a limited amount of actions and have to figure out how to maximise your score.

In the last round of the game there are only scoring goals on display and player bet (as they usually do in Keyflower) to get those scoring tiles that fit them best. A very neat concept because you have to diversify in different paths from the beginning of the game to keep your options open in this last round.

RATING: 9
The central auction / resource management ideas in this game is really interesting and fun.  Basically you start with 8 random meeples of different currencies and when you bid on a tile with one or more meeples of the same colour you set that as the currency for that tile.  Equally that tile can be played on as a resource as long as there is colour consistency.  It is an auction game matched with a tile placement game with a little pick up and deliver.  It does reward knowing the tiles so it is a fun game for the first time you play but can see this getting more interesting if you are playing this with the same group multiple times.  In future plays I will enjoy the different flavour that the different seasons have but in first game this just upped the strategy learning curve.  Its certainly fun to play.  I have the merchants expansion and like what it adds to the game.  It makes it more beginner friendly because there are more ways to get points including ways that other people cannot outbid you for.  Wouldnt want to play every game with the expansion stuff but adding the tiles to the mix will create the right blend for me.

RATING: 8
:d10-3:
Great design. Poor development. Nothing is technically wrong with the development, but two choices stick out as particularly annoying to me.

One, the hidden information. Besides the obvious no-no of HBTI, the strategy, tactics, and brinkmanship are severely hampered by playing blindly. I fixed that by always playing without the player shields. MUCH improved.

Two, the whole geographical portion of the game - tile placement, transportation of resources, and some victory point tiles. It doesn't really add anything to the game, as you can't plan for this feature during the main portion of the game, yet you have to devote a portion of your brain to figuring out optimal placement and movement of resources at various parts of your turn. The game would be shortened by ~25% and be focused on the really good parts of the game if this whole geographical portion had been excised. There's no house rule fix for this, but I wish buildings and resources had been dealt with like Le Havre.

As long as you're playing with open information, the wonderful use of keyples as resources is worth dealing with the bad parts of the game. 

12/8/2012: Feels a little like Glen More's big brother, with the tile placement and resource production. The keyples, through the bidding and the action placement, are easily the best part of the game. I can't wait to try this with 3 or 4 players.

RATING: 6
Pretty good game.  Falls into my "simple but elegant" game list because the rules feel easy/intuitive enough to play weeks/months apart without much of a rules refresher, yet there are lots of options on your turns (especially after the first of four rounds/seasons).  I like the tough choices of how high to start the bidding and which color meeple (money) to bid with since it sets the auction color.

UPDATE 2016: you can get some really weird tile distributions, especially with 3p... we had a game with no buildings for moving goods besides the home-base tiles, and it was a very weird game.  There's just too much uncertainty in the game for me, particularly with picking tiles for the last round where you can guess really well or guess poorly and get creamed by someone who saved the right stuff for a tile you thought was safe to include in the final round.

RATING: 8
Fully expecting to hate this after the rules explanation (and that wasn't the fault of the explainer!) - sounded like a typical euro worker placement game.

I was wrong - it's a pleasantly vicious worker placement/auction game, where you use your workers as currency to bid on the tiles, and once you have the tiles you can use them yourself for 'free' or use other players at the cost of losing the worker(s) to them.

Like it ...

RATING: 8.5
Juego de losetas y colocación de trabajadores. En cada turno, los jugadores pujan por obtener ciertas losetas. La moneda para las pujas son los propios trabajadores. Una vez que se resuelven las pujas, las losetas te permiten construir tu ciudad, donde se generan recursos que cada uno tiene que desplazar a los sitios donde hagan falta para convertirlos en otros recursos, mejorar la loseta, etc.
La enjundia del juego está en los combos que se pueden obtener combinando las losetas. Está muy bien. Escala estupendamente. Es interesante y jugable de 2 a 5 jugadores.
Muy, pero que muy recomendable.

RATING: 9
Outstanding rules set with intuitive gameplay in the four seasons (rounds). Just the right amount of options for worker placement to make this an appealing game for most gamers. I like the size of the tiles which allows for easy access to information. The game allows for flexibility with workers that have become useless so players can find alternate uses for them. Bidding for the first player tile forces a choice between going first for the boat of worker/skills, or playing first in the next round, to make even that decision sometimes a tough one. This is an excellent design of balanced gameplay, and one that should have a great deal of replayability since you won't see all the village tiles in the same game. Top notch game.

RATING: 7.5
For a game that looks like a dry, boring euro, this one has impressed me. This is partly due to it's interaction, which is so delicious, and a great selling point for this one. Bidding games and worker placement games tend to work well with me, so in a way it's no surprise that I like it, but this really is something of a unique beast. It feels quite different than other medium euros, and yet it's simple enough for you to grasp after a round of play, making it feel very rewarding after only ten minutes or so. One strike against it is the theme. The mechanisms are actually fairly thematic for a euro, but the theme that's there is also quite dull so it definitely does feel dry. Nonetheless, I enjoyed my first play a great deal and look forward to giving it another try.

This is the R&D version of the game.

On 2/19/2013 I finally brought this game to the table after owning it for 6 months. I need to teach it at a convention this weekend so I had to get atleast one play in before then. 

Going into the first play and the initial reading of the rules I wasn't expecting a whole lot. I primarily bought the game for the collectibility not the game play. To say I was plesently suprised by the game play would be a bit of an understatment. What I initailly thought was just a pretty box full of fantastic components turned into an intrigueing Auction/Worker Placement/Tile Laying game that really works pretty nicely. It's going to need a few more play throughs to see if there is really enough strategy and replayability for this game to raise above a 7 but I still enjoyed it.

Update - 2/26/2013

Played this at a local convention this weekend with a full group. The strategy is definatly there. The second playthrough saw me actually working towards a planned end goal. Ultimatly though I came in second to a guy scoring a ton of points off of skills.

RATING: 8.5
Really an amazing design. Since each game uses a different set of tiles and has a different set of victory conditions, every game you play requires you to build an entirely new resource/worker/VP engine. I love that you can use your opponents' buildings if you need them (and can really screw up their plans by doing so). The bidding is also really tense, made even more stressful by the addition of the green workers—a brilliant touch. Definitely one of the best worker-placement games I've played, with one of the best auction systems to boot. Plays great with two.

:thumbsup: [b]POSITIVE POINTS:[/b]

++ Great, medium-heavy-weight auction-bidding/tile-laying/worker placement strategy-heavy game with a lot of path to victory, where you are building your worker placement tableau over the course of the game (not the same fix worker placement board to use for the whole game), and where you bid with your worker (they are thus also kind of a currency).
++ Great sense of escalation from the first season where you can only bid on a few tiles, to the last season where you are bidding and activating so much tiles to fulfill your strategy.
++ I like that you have the possibility to play your workers on the opponent's tableau (but thus giving them your workers) and that you also have the possibility to play your workers on the center tiles available for bid (that are still to no one yet).
++ I love that you can upgrade your tiles, by paying ressources needed, and then by just flipping it (and that your tableau slowing change season, from spring/summer green tree to autumn colored leave).
++ I like the idea of having to move resources from where they are made to where they are needed.
++ I find really interesting that we can pass during the auction phase, and then continue playing if somebody haven't pass. Great bluffing ability.
++ There are expansions available for this game (which is useful to refresh the game when we have overplayed it).
++ I like that The Farmers expansion, especially when playing the official Farmer variant (with all Farmer tiles), really change de game strategies without changing the gameplay. It's refreshing and my wife particularly love this expansion.

:thumbsdown: [b]NEGATIVE POINTS:[/b]

-- This game is really prone to Analysis Paralysis (AP), as there is so many choices, especially as the game progress, so turn can sometimes be long (especially the first turn of a new season, where you have to make a strategy for the season: what you are targeting for, what you could upgrade, how to do all this, what's your plan B, etc.).
-- This is probably the limit of brain-burning my wife is confortable to accept, so she must be in the mood for it (thus this game is harder to get to the table).
-- This game has a bit too much players interaction for my wife.
-- Harder and longer to teach to new people.
-- When using The Farmers expansion, the game is more fiddly to play because there are so much meeple/animeeple on player's tiles, so upgrading them, or just reading them at a distance is harder.

RATING: 8
Hits a sweet spot for me, as I really enjoy engine-building games, and this one has it in spades combined with a nice combo of auction (which I'm generally not a fan of, but is implemented very well here) and worker placement.

In Keyflower, workers are your currency, and they come in three different colors.  You are trying to build up a village full of useful abilities, using your men to both bid on new tiles (to get more abilities and points) while also using your men to activate the special abilities of the tiles.

The really interesting thing here is that once one color of men is used to activate or bid on a tile, only that color of men may be used to do further bidding on or activating of that tile.  So, if someone puts a blue worker on a tile to activate it, and you were hoping to bid on that tile but have no blue workers, your out of luck, and they just blocked you.  This means there is a ton of player interaction, but at the same time it doesn't feel overly confrontational.

In the last round of the game, all of the tiles are tiles that score players bonus points rather than having more abilities.  Each player knows a handful of these tiles that might come out, so during the first 3/4 of the game, you work toward these goals since they are a lot of the points that will be scored.  So, you'll be trying to focus on a set of tiles with abilities that allow you to exploit these last round tiles.

Overall, just a great mash-up of engine building, auction (but not your standard auction!), and worker placement, with many strategies and avenues to explore.

RATING: 7
[floatleft][ImageID=1402873][/floatleft][size=11][b][thing=122515][/thing][/size][/b] is on the lighter side of the [family=48][/family] titles I've encountered.  That's not to say it's shallow, however.  In fact, I believe [thing=122515][/thing] has quite a bit of potential, the extent of which is not yet apparant after just one play (a two-player affair at that). 

The core of the game is a four-round auction for building tiles that, when won, are added to a player's individual village.  The tiles generally either produce generic resources or permit you to translate those resources into victory points.  With the addition of restrictions on tranporting goods between tiles, the construction of buildings in a players' village is a logistical puzzle reminscent of [thing=12681][/thing].  The key (no pun intended) is that players can simultaneously participate in the auction (paid for in workers) while employing their workers to activate any of the buildings on the table.  The resultant play provides an interesting sequencing puzzle intertwined with a highly clever mechanism for re-allocating the game's fundamental currency.[floatright][imageID=1402899][/floatright]

Notwithstanding its positives, aspects of [thing=122515][/thing] fell flat in my first game.  First is that the game is highly random.  Workers and other beneficial tiles are blindly drawn, as are the tiles eavailable each game. Additionally, by the fourth season (of four), I began to feel overly distracted the production and scoring opportunities.  It felt like our game tipped by the end from something clever to yet-another generic efficiency puzzle.  If you are a fan of those aspects of [thing=66362][/thing] and [thing=70149][/thing], [thing=122515][/thing] is certainly worth a look as a novel twist on similar ideas (in a beautiful package, as should go without saying).

RATING: 9
A meaty, strategically satisfying Euro with HIGH player interaction and little downtime? Yessssss.

This is kind of a rough game to learn. It's easy enough to understand (but not master) the systems in place here, but there's a reliance on familiarity with the content of the game that makes it tricky. Whenever we introduce this to people they're always confused about how to score until the very end of the game... which is when you find out how to score.

Thankfully the game moves at a nice brisk pace and has really compelling mechanics so people are generally happy enough to put up with the initial confusion.

There's a lot to consider with each turn, but not so much that it feels overwhelming. There's some minor aspects of bluffing and deduction as well, which I really enjoy. Nothing feels better than laying low, waiting for someone to pass, then swooping in and stealing a tile from them. Yesssssss, delicious pain.

It is really a brutal game. Not just in that it's competitive, but every choice is really, really tough. It's hard to scrape together enough workers to bid with, so every placement feels exceedingly important.

The only qualm I have is some of the tiles could be clearer - there are a couple aspects of the game feel unintuitive, but I really believe it's mostly an issue of how certain information is presented (i.e., that moving goods onto the winter tiles works differently etc.)

That said, esoteric iconography is my largest recurring complaint in games and though the tiles aren't pretty, we were able to figure out what almost all of them meant at first glance, so it's really a minor issue.

It also feels like it runs a round too short (sometimes you can pay through the nose for something only to never get to use it) - it always ends JUST when my engine gets going. But at least the upside is it always leaves you hungry for more!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Keyflower features a mix of auction, worker placement and spatial element – lovely! It’s a very good game, but after 3 plays I decreased my rating by 0.5. Why? Because the game ends too soon! It feels like you don’t have time to build up your engine, and in autumn and winter you’re rushing to activate a lot of your tiles in order to get VPs. It almost seems as if the game would have been better if it lasted for 6 seasons instead of 4… And there are certainly not many games which would get better by an extended playing time, that’s for sure! I and the rest of the players don’t feel you had time to do much if anything – and this isn’t meant in the good way as in Agricola, where you can never do anything you want. Here, there isn’t that much interesting stuff to do I think, and the seasons end too rapidly so you don’t really get to do much during the game. 

Perhaps this has something to do with the fact that I’ve only played it with 2 and 3 players, but I cannot imagine 5-6 players would increase my enjoyment and fix my issues with the game; on the contrary, I think it would bog down with many players, and only the auction part of the game would perhaps be slightly more interesting. Not that the auction doesn’t work with fewer players, it actually works very well which I was surprised by.

I normally like games in which I place my tiles in certain patterns to benefit the most from them, and while this game features this mechanic, I didn’t think it was that exciting or well-deployed. Partly because you don’t get that many tiles and partly because you always know how many roads the tiles have in each season. 

What I did like about the tiles is that only part of them come out each turn, so each game might be vastly different from the previous one, so the replayability is great (though I do need to say that many of the tiles are almost the same, but just concern the different kinds of resources etc). I also loved the fact that you get some secret winter goal tiles before the game, of which you choose at least one which is then auctioned off in the winter, so you can make a strategy about these tiles; just be sure you actually win them… Oh, and this is probably one of my favorite things about the game, and what makes the game feel new and refreshing: how you bid for tiles. You don’t have a specific player color, but instead you make a bid on a tile with one of four colored meeples, and then everybody else has to use that same color if they want to outbid you! You can also use the same color to activate a tile which is on auction, but the winner of the tile is going to get that meeple for the next round! The same applies with tiles in your opponent’s villages; you can use them, but if you do so, they will keep the meeples for the next round, so it’s always a hard decision what to do!

Keyflower is definitely a game I would be willing to play quite a bit, but is it really one I need to own when I own 200 games? I don’t think so, and neither does my wife, whose main critique equals mine; the game ends way too fast before you feel you have done anything.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
There is an interesting meeple management aspect going on in this game. You need to keep your stock replenished so that you can do actions and bid on new action tiles, but you lose them when you do actions or bid on new tiles, unless the tiles belong to you. You replenish you guys by having others use your tiles, bidding on tiles that contain meeples, and a certain income of meeples per turn. The cool thing is they come in three colors and once you start a bid/action of that color, the other colors are locked out from that space.  Another strength of the game is it plays quite quickly with only 4 rounds. It feels very polished and the decisions are interesting. I wasn't a big fan of the carcassone style of tile laying; it just seemed superfluous to what the game was doing

Simple bidding and home town development game.  Get the  feeling with mixing between Carcassonne and Lehavre.  Just like it!

Keyflower…人有人緣棋有棋緣, 若單從用機制去判定Keyflower 好壞實在不易, 在Euro game 而言說機制Keyflower著實有太多缺點 , 例如 太隨機性的抽取人種 (Meeples 的顏色); 一場遊戲中你若有不濟的人種組合只有問蒼天的份兒, 抽取tile 也是隨機的; 若有不符合你預期長遠目標的tile 或給對手用你沒有的人種搶之而後快, 你一定哭訴無門。

無疑遊戲令玩家把planning 的strategy 也變成機動的tactics。本應不大討好的Euro game元素 混在一起, 理應不堪入目, 但設計師竟可運用這些元素發揮神奇的果效, 遊戲中演變成有機動策略的刺激, 把bidding 機制展现得精采, 正面的衝突也被清新的畫風淡化。 這keyflower 的作者也許刻意步處玩家要從不確定性中找取機動策略, 從而得到滿足。你若問我Keyflower好玩嗎? 我會毫不猶豫的說好, 但我會補充一句問你是否接受它的隨機元素, 若你接受, 它便與你有緣。看今天從Keyflower在BGG的排名27來看, 此棋著實有棋緣。

RATING: 7
This game folds all of the interesting, interactive bits of euro engine building games into one very curious package that's like nothing before it.  In other words, it's asking familiar questions but in very different ways.  I can imagine this game getting really, really interesting at high levels of play and it's likely a denser, deeper title than Terra Mystica.  

Still, I've got my worries.  I don't know if I am yet playing it correctly.  I don't know if the hidden information element will turn the game into a crap shoot or if such things can be puzzled.  I don't know if, at high player counts, the game will simply become too unstable for any level of strategic planning.

As is though it is remarkable and an enjoyable two player game.  This one starts at 8 and could go as high as 9.

Update.  After about 10 plays I put this one the auction block. It's a fine game but largely a missed opportunity. Certain elements felt undercooked and missed their chance at something bigger.  Still, I loved the action/auction system and multiple currencies.  But, especially with repeated plays, there's just too much to track and too many micro exchanges.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Typical 'hasty' or 'frantic' growth engine and mild resource conversion game where you have tons of options and too little time to make a meaningful choice. Keyflower therefore joins the ranks of Homesteaders and Factory Manager, and in some more remote way Lancaster and World Without End. The game is crafted thusly that every round a careful selection of buildings, fitting the general game state at that moment, enters the proceedings: resources first, then transportation and special abilities, then the first singular VP tiles, and finishing off with a crescendo finale where Big VP are to be had. The only snag is that you have no idea whether tiles you select or bid on in the beginning have any redeeming feature for the future seasons, rendering the affair rather more tactical than need me, methinks. It is therefore a smash hit with the poor sods who confuse depth and meaningful gameplay with having tons of options. As for myself: this sort of game is only worth my time if I were to play it on dozens of occasions so I would get a feel for the possible combinations, and frankly the prospect bores me. 

Are there any redeeming features to Keyflower? Yes, I think so. There is a shadow of a memory of goods transportation in this game which serves as a bit of an artificial bottleneck, but still. And the use of coloured meeples is clever, especially considering the presence of a 4th colour with which players can be really screwed over. That is an unexpected touch of interaction in an otherwise dry and dull game. I'm generous with my rate as a consequence.

RATING: 10
Amazing original game.  I was blown away after my first play of Keyflower.  The game is simple yet woven with layers of complexity.  The bidding angle keeps the game constantly interesting.  Is there enough for one more big move?  Can I outbid and steal the tile back from the center?  Should I use my worker one someone else's tile (they get the worker the following turn)?  The replayability of Keyflower is tremendous. My experiences have been with either 4 or 6 players around the table.  And the expansions "The Merchants" and "The Farmers" are fantastic additions to the base game and I highly recommend integrating both expansions for a truly great game!

I may be wrong about this, but I had the hardest time understanding it my first game, and even after that I found it hard to come up with a strategy at all my first few games. These are some reasons for the above mentioned learning curve. 

All in all, not a bad game if you are into it. I just don't like it enough to buy it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Best game I have ever played.  Brilliant mix of mechanics including worker placement, auction bidding, pick up and deliver, resource management, set collecting, and tile placement. The theme is also very cute and colorful.  You're trying to build the best little village.  Gameplay is SO elegant, all you will ever do on a turn is work a tile or bid.  Every game plays different because of the variable set up for each round.  On top of that each player is given hidden scoring goals to lay out at the last round.   There are a lot of strategies to pursue.  You can try to max out a scoring goal tile, you can go crazy on upgrading your entire village, or just try to get a mix of bonuses.  This game is no multiplayer solitaire game, it's very interactive and you'll constantly be engaged with your opponents trying to outbid them or work a tile so you can shut them out.  You may even send your workers to their village to get a benefit from a tile they own (although they will get your workers).  This game is also amazing with all player counts.  It's just as good as it with 2 players as it is with 6 players.  That's insane!  How many games scale that well.  Really I can't say enough great things about Keyflower.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Can't say I have run into anything quite like this. A fun, if mildly brainy game.  It doesn't touch the heaviness of our heavy euros (Agricola, Terra Mystica, etc), but it still leaves you thinking due to the strong interaction between players.

The bid/activation colour locking mechanism is where your strategy comes into play.  You have random colours, so do your opponents.  You have to assume you know where you have the edge and play from there, expect surprises unless you want to devote time to diligently counting meeples (from an unknown start point, and potentially changing perspective).  

The most interesting mechanic is the trade between activating tiles outside your village, as you are giving (directly) or potentially giving other players meeple for next round.  This means you really need to have those tiles, but when the bids start to fly you won't get everything you want.  Makes for some very interesting play.

You get to build your own little village more or less but are constantly away of others' and can use them (a la La Havre).  There's a lot of outguessing going on in this one and the fact that the currencies are hidden makes this one very good.

RATING: 7
One reviewer strongly suggests the house rule: "Open Information Stays Open and Closed Information Stays Closed."

Another adds: we've started going one further than this house rule: at the beginning of the game, we lay out all of the Summer and Autumn tiles that will arrive. Again, it might sound like this could make the game super-prone to AP, but it didn't at all for us (3p). The main thing it provides is a quick sense of the relative values of gold, iron, wood, and stone for the game, rather than randomly finding those out as the game unfolds (which we found unsatisfying).

RATING: 9
Five games now done (only one win). Very impressed. Wildly varying scores ... as high as 80 (huge win by Scott) down to the low 20s. Seems like the 50s is where the winning mostly happens. I like it very much, and was a bit taken aback at how much fun I had playing this game. I have to think about the strategy of the game a bit more, but there is something about playing the game that is just pure fun for me. I finish one game and literally want to jump into another one, and that is a rare thing.

Keyflower has really grown on me. It's delightful and quick-ish to play, the game is easy to explain and to pick up, it's a challenge to play well, and also offers immense replayability. [b]I find this game joyful to play[/b]. Watch out for first-time player AP. Probably best to play with no more than 4 when teaching the game.

This one's brilliantly fun and clearly needed a "9" rating from me.

[b]I own the following expansion(s) for the game:[/b]

Keyflower: The Farmers
Keyflower: The Merchants (thank you Secret Santa 2014)

Both are rare examples of expansions that wonderfully expands one of my favourite games. Elegant, fun, great addition, and most importantly simple to add without adding chrome or extra time to the gameplay. Expansions I rate as highly as the original base game. Both are highly recommended if you love Keyflower.

Thanks to Richard for mailing it over from the UK.

[b]I also own the expansion/promo tiles:[/b]

Emporium, Monument, Storyteller, Beekeeper, Key Celeste

RATING: 9

RATING: 8.5
The design of this game is absolutely inspired. For my money, the simplicity-to-strategic-depth ratio of this game is second to none. What you can do on your turn is fairly straightforward. What you should do (and when you should do it, and using which meeples, and in what quantity) is anything but. The unique combination of two typically separate and rarely equal mechanisms--worker placement and tile auction--make both exponentially better. I just can't say enough good about how this game plays.

My only complaints are that it's thematically very dry, a bit ugly, and the rulebook really sucks. And, at lower player counts, it can be frustrating how many tiles are removed from the game, as some players will randomly find their villages in a much better position than others in the late stages of the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game has intrigued me since I saw it for a first time. Back then, I didn't like such type of euro-economic games as they seemed dry, lifeless and without any player interaction. Yet something made Keyflower stick in my mind - something unique was there. Nowdays I have become more open to various genres of games, including not only ameritrash/area control, but euro as well - and Keyflower has been released in Polish so nothing could stop me from buying. 

Oh boi, this is so magnificent. An unusual blend of different mechanics - bidding, worker placement, resource production and developing a village. Technically, you're doing only two actions the whole game: either you bid or you produce. The thing is that you do both using meeples(which come in four colours)and it resolves all around that. Here comes probably the most important rule: once a meeple(or meeples) has been assigned to a tile you and other players must follow that color. So if my opponent starts with blue, if I want to outbid him, I have to use blue as well. Man, so simple rule, yet it creates so many possibilites and tactical play, not to mention a memory and guessing element - "hmm, how many of these could he have remaining?". If you manage to solve that puzzle you'll be able to add tiles won during the bidding to your village. It gives you access to various producing actions, which usually give you more meeples, resources or skill tokens. Every tile can be upgraded in order to become more powerful and - finally - give victory points. I haven't mentioned the fact that you can use tiles in other players' villages - as long as you follow the proper colour, of course. You can also block them this way, because village tiles have a certain limit of meeples that may be put on them - and trust me, sometimes it encourages to do very nasty things... but it doesn't come without a payoff, it's a double edged sword - you give precious meeples(again, the most important resource in this game to be honest)to your enemy for the next round. Keeping a balance between bidding for tiles that might be useful, using them, going to your opponent's villages, sometimes blocking important spots and keeping track of how many meeples you give away... it creates a really big field for player interaction and opportunities to perform surprising plays. And I haven't even mentioned about managing your village logistically - creating a proper net of roads is extremely important and if you make too many mistakes here it will definitely affect you negatively during the next rounds - all because in order to upgrade not only you must have proper resources, but they have to be delivered directly to a tile you want to upgrade. And without upgrading it's impossible to think about the victory.

An excellent, unusual, innovative game, twisting and blending various mechanisms that work together perfectly. A brilliantly designed product.

RATING: 8.2
Each player builds up a village by bidding on tiles with workers. These tiles can be used to perform certain actions (produce resources, transport resources and upgrade your village tiles, attract new workers, obtain human capital). One cannot only use one's own tiles, but also the tiles in other villages and even the tiles which are for sale. This does imply that your worker will join the work force of your adversary for the next round (season) or the work force of the player who buys the new tile. So, in each of the four seasons you have to make hard choices of what to do with your workers, bid on new tiles or use tiles. And there are only four seasons... At the start of the game each player also receives a number of tiles (2 or 3) which they can put up for sale in the winter. These tiles describe conditions to be met in order to score points. The player can use these tiles to guide his own strategy, but it is uncertain if he will be able to obtain the tiles. Points can be scored in many different ways. A very nice worker placement game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
EDIT: After a second play, bumped to 8.5... after a 3rd play, bumped to 9. This is just an excellent game. Everyone who has played has loved it and wanted to play again (and again).
- - -
UPDATE: Every session of this has been just great. Bumping it up (again), to a 9.5.
- - -
There's some really great innovation here. The game is essentially about worker placement (of a very creative sort), resource conversion, tile placement and auctions... but the interplay is definitely unique and superbly implemented.

The "currency" you use in the auctions is workers and since there are three (or four) different colors of workers, that effectively means there are three (or four) different currencies. Once someone bids in red workers, no other color of workers can be bid for that item.

Using production tiles is similar. Once someone has used a blue worker on a tile, if you want to use that tile you have to use blue as well (and you have to place more workers than the previous player). Likewise, if you want to use a tile that has received bids--or vice versa--you must match the color of workers/bids already at that tile.

A lot of sneaky tile placement things occur, too... like the fact that when you use someone else's building you are giving them workers (currency) for next round, since workers in each village are returned to the village's owner at the end of every season. The bids for claiming start player and re-supply ships have some clever twists as well.

Simply put, this is just a very well-designed game. It will take many plays to get a handle on how to play it well... and my rating could easily increase. (Note: HAS increased... started at 8.0, now 9.5.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Players are developing land in the New Keyworld, trying to amass the most victory points.

Intriguing resource management game.  Players on their turn deploy meeples from behind their house to either bid on a tile, or activate any tile for production.  Meeples come in four colours, and once a tile has been used, it's locked into that colour for the remainder of the turn.  Tiles that are won are bolted into the player's own play area at the end of the turn.  Tiles produce goods, more meeples, craft tokens, and of course, victory points.

The game is played over four seasons, bubbles along nicely, and has some very interesting design features.  You want good, attractive, tiles so the other players will come and give you meeples, but you want them for yourself too.  

Really enjoyed this one - by far the best game in the Key series.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very interesting twist on worker placement/bidding game as you need to figure out when it's best to activate a tile (whether your own or not) and when to bid for one. The random selection of tiles in each season makes for great replay value.

RATING: 7
Purchased at favorite local game store (04-08-2013); a very interesting game.  We really like the goofy way you use your workers to bid for things.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Keyflower is one of those games I'm kind of ambivalent about.  There's nothing inherently wrong with it (although a few things bug me, as we'll get to below), but I'm never going to be excited to play it.  It has a lot of interesting points, as you try to build up your little network of tiles, produce goods, and transport them from place to place.

However, I feel like you really just need to grab as many meeples as you can, to win auctions and activate tiles.  Being able to activate other players' tiles is a good addition, but not something to do unless you're desperate, since you have to give them the workers.  Start player for the next round is pretty important, since you can get the biggest boat (unless you really need one of the others).  I also think it's weird that there's one special color.  Having a lead in green the whole game means you can just take whatever tile you want, defeating the whole purpose of the auction part of the game.
That's the disconnect for me.  You've got to manage your own empire, but then fight with everyone else over the parts to add to it.  If people decide to contest you, you can waste a lot of time and resources attempting to secure these tiles.  
You get 2 end-game scoring tiles at the start which you can build towards.  But then, you're under no obligation to put them out for auction.  And even if you do put them out, there's no guarantee you'll win them.  So you've not only got to set up the victory conditions on your tiles, but get a big enough staff of meeples that you can defend them in the final auction.  It also really seems to me (and the other people I've played with) that some of these endgame tiles have way higher scoring potential than others, and some are much easier to complete than others.  Getting stuck with the keythedral (an unexciting flat 12 points) and a tile that rewards you for collecting meeples (which everyone is going to be competing for anyway) in your opening hand means that you're going to be in a much worse position than someone who's trying to collect a bunch of skill tokens.  If you put your tiles out, you'll have to try and stop other players from poaching them (since they're as good for others as they are for you), but if you don't, you'll have to fight an uphill battle to steal someone else's tile, which probably won't score as well.
There are also some publicly available tiles, where you want to make road connections, or get boats or something, but these are difficult to build towards and give a piddly amount of points even if you're able to do it and get your hands on the right tile.

Anyway, If the game were more about the network and engine building, and less about oddball auctions and the buildup towards these wonky endgame bonuses, I'd like it more.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.9
You have an interesting decision to make every turn.  

This is a great four currency money management game in which the goal of the game is to get the most points, not the most money (although you can get points for certain types of coin collections if you put in the winning bid on certain winter tiles).  This land uses meeple shaped coins for its four distinct, inconvertible* currencies.  Keeping track of multiple inconvertible currencies is challenging as most players of this game can probably attest, and perhaps that is why the residents responsible for each tile refuse to have to deal with more than one of the currencies per season as long as they can get away with it (note: Boats 4a and 4b come with government-enforced privileges).

Each time around the table you can pass or you can allocate some of your money (to buy price-capped progressively more expensive goods, to pay for price-capped progressively more expensive services or to bid on businesses, privileges or other investment opportunities up for auction, most of which you can teleport to your part of town, including the surrounding land, roads and water if you put in the winning bid).  You have so many options to consider on your turn, you can even bid on getting to pick which available income (including a tool or two) you will receive at the end of the season.  This totally feels like bribing a government official (a kickback).  The different incomes the players can receive are displayed in boats right from the start of the season, not to encourage the players to pour more money into developing their villages, but on the contrary one can only assume it is done by someone in the government in charge of allocating government funds to the players, and it is done to encourage the players to instead use some of their money to bribe that government official into letting them choose which income they will receive.  The government official who displayed the different income options on the boats divided the incomes unevenly, and it seems self evident that this was done in order to maximize the bribes he/she will receive from the players for getting to choose which income they will receive.  At the end of the season, the places no one decided to bid on presumably get burnt down and any money inside them is repossessed by the government.

Winning the game isn't necessarily about having the best village.  Developing a nice village filled with nice, renovated places capable of providing lots of goods and useful services certainly helps a lot, because the government definitely finds out about those things, but there are also government officials who start accepting bribes at the start of winter and the player who makes the best offer to one of those government officials gets to transport that government official (including the building they typically reside in) back to their village for the official to check out the village for the one specific kind of thing (or set of things) they care about (One of them just cares about the building he/she typically resides in, the keythedral.).  Each player meets and gets to know a few government officials at the start of the game (winter tiles) so they know exactly what those particular government officials value.  At the start of winter, each player must summon one and can summon more than one of those government officials they met at the start of the game to be available to perform an inspection of a village at the end of the game.  All players have a chance to chat with those selected government officials to get to know exactly what they value right before winter starts.

So yeah... this game is pretty thematic. :)

Most things seem pretty clear, but one thing I'm wondering about is... when I pay to use the alehouse, tavern or the inn, am I paying for a drink or a room for the night and then selling my sexy body to a client who pays in whatever currency they happen to have on them?  Is that why I get paid and why I get paid more when I use the nicer, upgraded versions of these places due to the clientele I can attract in them? It should be noted that these clients appear to be spending money they've obtained from the government.  Are they bandits or are they perhaps government officials spending the government's money for their own pleasure? Also, the payments made by the players for getting to pick which income they will receive end up in the government's coffer. I suspect there is some sort of money laundering scheme going on which involves the official who displays the incomes on the boats, but I haven't gotten to the bottom of it yet.


*For a price-capped progressively more expensive service fee, the fair, peddler and store each allow players to exchange one unit of one of the other currencies for one or two units of the green currency.  This may be some sort of (price-capped) black market.


+ The Merchants
