RATING: 9
A really advanced worker placement (& worker auction?) with numerous paths to victory, although it is quite challenging for new players to understand the game flow. Tiles are used to activate abilities and generate resources. Players spend meeples to use these tiles when they are in the current pool, or they bid on those tiles to bring them into their own towns for the next season where they can be used without losing meeples. All owned tiles can be used by other players and the owner keeps the spent meeples. Each game randomly generates different resource shortages and the 'most valuable' tiles seem to change from game to game. A bit complicated for beginners, but I believe there's a great deal of replay value, especially between skilled opponents. This is my least played 10 but it deserves it!

90-120m, 2-4 players. 5+ plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Keyflower is a game of auctions, resource acquisition, end game scoring bonuses and a small spatial element with the tiles you acquire.  There are 4 rounds, with each round being associated with a season.  During each season tiles will be up for auction.  They do have a pattern:

Spring: Resource acquisition tiles
Summer: Boats which provide permanent special abilities and tiles which allow you to spend resources to gain more/more valuable resources.
Fall: Scoring opportunity tiles by spending or moving resources.
Winter: End of game scoring bonuses for resources you save until the end of the game.

The resources come in two main types: Meeples and Non-Meeples (which there are multiple types). The way meeples are used to both activate tiles and bid on tiles is the most interesting part of the game.  The auction and use of tiles are all happening simultaneously in a round.  To elaborate, when you bid or activate a tile you use a meeple/meeples of the same color which locks the tile into that color for counterbids and activations.  Not only can you activate other people's tiles (essentially giving them your meeples) but you can activate tiles that are being bid on (meaning people bid not only on the tile, but the meeples that activated the tiles).  

Winter Tiles are also more unique than the other seasons.  At the beginning of the game players are dealt winter tiles.  At the beginning of the 4th round player's secretively and simultaneously choose how many and which tiles they want to add to Winter's auction.  After choosing the tiles, they are shuffled and displayed so people aren't 100% sure who added what tiles to the auction.  The first problem I have is: either you are either forced to specialize in the winter tiles you've been dealt or risk them not being in the game.  This is a minor complaint as the same argument could be leveled at Troyes.  The more troubling issue for me is that in the 2 games I've played, it was almost more important to bid on winter tiles that seem likely to give the player a win than what would benefit you the most.  I would finally caution that you have to be very careful which axis you're flipping your tiles as their orientation matters.

This is a very interesting game that's going to appeal to many people but I didn't quite work for me for the two reasons I mentioned relating to the winter tiles.  Despite not using every tile in every game (increasing the games replay value), I think after repeated plays the game would start to feel samey for me.

[b]Status: TRADED for Tobago & Aton[/b]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.7
Keyflower is one of those games that can be easily unappreciated based on a player's expectations of the game. The first two games I played were terrible and I spent more time face deep in the rulebook than playing the game. Wife checked out in the first 15 minutes. We gave it another chance and realized how much amazing potential this game has. The first thing you have to realize going into this game is how passive aggressive the bidding system is which also builds necessary tension. Being able to pass and then continue playing allows for an interesting way to steal tiles last minute from your opponent, which is an absolutely necessary part of the game. Also, you have to realize how tight this game is. Every turn is bidding and producing (they are not separate phases) so you have to use your workers in such a way to build resources and also expand your village. However, workers are scarce and you end up having to spend more workers each time an action is used more than once. The thing to remember is that it can be cutthroat and also there will not be much time to do everything you want to do because there are only 4 rounds and workers are used for everything. Very tight, stressful game with a lot of tension. Easily one of the best games on the shelf.

RATING: 7
This game is pretty innovative. You bid on tiles by placing workers on the side, and you can use the same tiles by placing workers on top. Then there is the puzzle aspect after you win them with your bids. Auctions, worker placement, and puzzle aspects all combined make this an interesting game. I have mixed feelings about the winter tiles, since some of them are very powerful. On one hand if you get one of the powerful tiles you can position yourself to make good use of them, but if you don't prepare you will not be able to make much use of them and probably won't bid for them at the end. (10 points for each set of skill tiles, I'm looking at you.) If anything, the game is too short; I never finish what I want to do by the end of the game.

RATING: 4
Two plays
I feel like the mechanics in this game needed to be reined in somehow. Like many Breese creations, understanding the mercurial flow of resources is difficult: crops in Key Harvest, polyps in Reef Encounter, and now the meeples in this game. Most often meeples are burned up in bidding and any that end up coming back to you is almost a side effect. You mostly just make the best go of it given the meeples you end up with each round.

Then the skill tiles are thrown in as another resource to juggle. Then you've got village building and having to move resources around your village. And some tiles seem pretty pointless (trade a meeple for a random meeple?). THEN you select final-round bonus tiles to go out for bidding but you still have to fight/bid to get the tile(s) you may have been shooting for. Oy!

So, it's a lot to digest and always seems like an uphill struggle where you never really get into any groove since THE GAME IS ONLY FOUR ROUNDS! It all feels rather clunky and mostly like managed chaos.

I can't fathom adding any expansions to increase the shit to manage. There's already too much crammed into this game that ONLY LASTS FOUR ROUNDS!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Ye ghods but that's an over-written rules set. But once you get past the rules, the game play is very simple and clever. The new thing here is that when a meeple is placed as a worker or a bidder to a tile, all other meeples to that tile must be the same colour. That's a killer rule!

Players start with red/yellow/blue meeples, but can get green ones. So if you're the only player with one green meeple, if you use it as a first bid, nobody can outbid you. Or if you use it for production, no-one else can produce from that tile or if it's up for auction, bid on it.

The rest of the game play is standard worker placement, developmental, optimal scoring. Get stuff, use it to get VPs. But there's enough variety and especially inter-play between villages to make it a really engaging game.

But very long, 1 hour to read the rules, 3 hours playing. Fun, we really enjoyed it, lots to consider, but long for what it is.

RATING: 4
Having only play this 2p and 4p, I'll say that it offers tight decisions with limited variability, and is more interesting with more than 2p.

How did this just go from a 4 to a 10?

Then how did this go from a 10 to a 6.

This game has a lot going for it, but then some things I find. Thematically it well-executed, with different jobs resulting in different yields available each season. And you only have four seasons to get things done, which results in a tight game that really fits the idea of one year.

There is variability in available tiles from game to game, but not as much as I had hoped for. There is the unique meeple auction, but it's not as fun at 2p as I was hoping. The resources are a good thematic fit and provide interesting decisions when couples with the transporting mechanic, but the tool chits feel much more mechanical and less interesting, especially when players are encouraged to hoard them.

RATING: 8
3-24-2012. Ordered directly from Richard through email.  Will be printed in time for Essen 2012. preorder #552

Excellent resource game.  The 4th (last round) can really turn the game around.  Choices can be excruciating.  Failing to get skill tiles can really be a problem.  

There is a lot of clever strategy in this game involving play on another's tiles where not only do you reap benefits but also sabotage your opponent--but in doing so will forfeit a worker and possibly lose end game bonus points. Complicated possibilities in especially the last round. I also found unique Green workers to be extremely strategic with play and counter-play very interesting.

Good adaptation for different number of players.

RATING: 4
Overall, I find this boring.  It is a mechanical, generic euro about bidding for certain buildings, activating them a few times to get some resources, and setting yourself up for scoring at the end of 4 rounds.  The theme is practically non-existant.

There is a hidden economy in there, but you don't really know how to value things other than bidding the max if you absolutely need it.  In addition, you have to look around at other players tableaus across the table quite often to remember what buildings they have, especially after they've upgraded them.

The interesting decisions: what color meeple do i use, and do i activate a building or bid for one, big whoop.

From a strategy perspective, the first time I played I didn't know what I was doing and so had a sprawling city but couldn't move things at all.  In my second game I focused only on one resource type as well as scoring tiles that only used that resource.  I wound up winning over a couple of players that had a more sprawling city and multiple resources.

Pass

RATING: 9
A very unique amazing auction / worker placement game that exceeded my high expectations.  Each game can play out completely differently depending on which tiles come out, and it scales very well from 2-6 players.  Essentially, each tile is bid on using one of up to 4 different kinds of currency (4 colors of meeples), and once a bid starts with one type of currency, no other type can be used for that auction.  The is one of maybe 10 games I've ever played that made me go "wow" the first time I played it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Külcsín:
A doboz engem elsőre nagyon meglepett. Ennyire jelentéktelen, csúnya dobozt, nem tudom láttam-e már. Nem értem, hogy jutott eszükbe ennyire színtelenre csinálni.
Kibontva már kellemesebb látvány fogadja az embert, és kicsit értelmet nyer a doboz is. Rengeteg lapka, mindegyiken különböző rajz. Valahogy kicsit gyermeki, ám mégis profi. Hihetetlenül sok meeple és nem kevés nyersanyag, amik hatszög alapú hasábok. Külön említést érdemel, hogy minden paraván egy-egy teljesen egyedi házikót mintáz, aminek még beltere is részletesen meg van rajzolva, és mindben egészen más a berendezés. Van tégla-, fa-, kőház, de még vályog is nádtetővel (a kedvencem )

A lapkák designjával kapcsoltban van egy -két praktikus kifogásom:
1. Túl halványan látszik csak a rajz. Ami miatt nem jön le, hogy mennyire különböző minden épület, illetve nehezen követhető az utak illesztése
2. A különböző típusú lapkák designja túl egyforma, így kínkeserves őket szétválogatni.

A szabály:
A szabály angolul és németül is bekerült a dobozba. Annak ellenére, hogy a játék logikája kicsit csavaros, jól érthető. Azért számítsatok rá, hogy megfelelő rutinnal is kétszer legalább el kell olvasni, hogy elkezdjen összeállni a játék.

A játék menete:
A játék négy évszakból áll. Minden évszakban licitálásra bocsátunk 7-9 darab lapkát. Ezek a lapkák, gyakorlatilag munkáslelyezésre alkalmas akciómezők. A licit érdekessége, hogy 3+1 féle színű lehet részt venni benne. Ha egy lapkára mondjuk pirossal már licitált valaki, akkor azt csak pirossal (logikusan abból is többel, mint az előző licit) lehet felüllicitálni. A +1 szín a zöld, ami csak annyiban más, mint a többi, hogy nehezebb hozzájutni, így logikusan értékesebb, és könnyebb vele licitet nyerni. A licitálásra bocsátott lapkák köre minden játékban más, így elég nagynak érzem az újrajátszhatóságot.
A játékos a körében vagy licitál, vagy lapkát aktivál. Mindkettő akció ugyanolyan munkásokkal történik. Aktiválhatjuk az egyik saját lapunkat, vagy akár másét is. A kör végén minden aktiválásra elhasznált munkás a lapka tulajdonosánál köt ki, így nem akkora baj, ha más használja a féltve őrzött kis szénbányánkat. Érdekes mechanizmus, hogy lehetséges az éppen licitre bocsátott, senki földje lapkákat is aktiválni. Innen majd az az ember kapja meg a munkásokat, akié a lapka lesz a licit végén. Szintén érdekes, hogy egy mezőt akár többször (többen) is aktiválhatnak. Az első aktiválás után (a licithez hasonlóan) a következő aktiválóknak ugyanolyan színt kell használniuk, mint ami már van a lapkán, és szintén a licithez hasonlóan, legalább eggyel több embert kell tenni. Tehát ha valaki, mondjuk elvitt 2 fát, egy piros emberrel, akkor a következőnek már 2 piros embert kell ezért "fizetnie".
A játék végét a tél jelenti. Az itt szerezhető lapkák, mind pontot adnak különféle célokért. Itt is egy érdekes mechanizmus jön be: nem véletlenszerűen felcsapott lapokra folyik a licit, hanem mindenki kap a játék elején 2-3 darabot amit titokba tart. Így mindenkinek van egy kis szelet infója arról, mikért járHAT majd pont a játék végén. A téli kör elején titkosan beadjuk azokat a lapkákat, amiket úgy ítélünk, hogy számunkra előnyösek. A beadott lapkákra, ugyanúgy licitálás folyik, mint a többi évszakban. Tehát attól még, hogy én adtam be a "minden zöld emberért két pont jár" lapkát, még nem biztos, hogy meg is fogom tudni szerezni.
Ha ezzel a licit és aktiváláskörrel is végeztünk, akkor összeszámoljuk a pontokat és a játéknak vége.

Összegzés:
- A játék nekem nagyon tetszett. Nagyon sok opcióból választhatunk, amikor sorra kerülünk (emiatt erre hajlamosak beakadhatnak, és nagyon elnyúlhat a játék). Szóval van mélysége a játéknak.
- Nagy az újrajátszhatósági faktor, mivel mindig más lapkakombinációkból gazdálkodunk.
- Hangulatos grafika, jó minőségűek az alkatrészek
- Aránylag nagyfokú interakció (és kitolási faktor), a licitálás és az egymás lapjának használata miatt
- Jópár érdekes, fineszes mechanizmus
- A rengeteg opció ellenére nagyfokú kiegyensúlyozottság (az első játék végén kb. 20%-on belül voltak a pontok)

A játékidő valamivel több, mint 2 óra volt. Gyakorlatilag mindenki kezdő volt, és 4-en játszottunk. Ahhoz képest, hogy eléggé gondolkodós darab, valamiért engem nem annyira fárasztott le, mint a hasonlók. És ez nagy szó, mert általában az agyzsibbasztási faktor miatt, szoktak lekerülni a fajsúlyosabb darabok az asztalról.

u.i.:
Még egy érdekesség jutott eszembe a működésből:
A lapkákat tovább lehet fejleszteni. Ehhez bizonyos mennyiségű nyersanyagra van szükség. Igen ám, de ezeknek a nyersanyagoknak ott is kell lennie lapkán, viszont a termelő lapkák saját magukra termelik a nyersanyagokat. Itt jön be a képbe a város okos építgetésének, illetve az utak a fontossága. A nyersanyagokat bizony szállítás akciókkal el kell juttatni a felhasználási helyükre, ami elég sok plusz fejvakarást visz a játékba. 

8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Keyflower clearly is part of the Key series. Unfortunately, that also means my general complaints about the other games in the series (that I have played) apply here as well.

Once again, there are some interesting mechanisms at work with a few innovative twists (although some aren't quite as new as some seem to think, e.g. Asara has already done the worker placement in specified colours thing before) but the end result feels like a pile of nice ideas that would have benefitted from some refinement. In particular, the game provides a host of opportunities for micromanagement (and, in fact, very much requires it) but lacks almost any sense of progression or achievement. Additionally, a large part of those management efforts can easily be rendered moot by the miniscule ripples caused by random draws. It's almost like you are pushing cubes for the sake of pushing cubes at times. The result can feel rather swingy and arbitrary because of this.

Also as usual, the graphic design is not up to par with the illustrations.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Relatively simple rules, but a whole lot of depth. Replayability is high since your strategy changes each game. You plan based on your winter tiles, but you ultimately don't know what you'll end up with by the end of the game.

The auction/action mechanic with the meeples is very clever. Trying to decide what to do with your own pool of meeples is a challenge, but you're also trying to maximize the size/color distribution of your own pool while minimizing your opponents'. Do I bid on that tile Player B wants in the hopes that she'll waste more meeples on it? Or do I bid on the tile I want with green meeples before somebody else can bid on it with yellows?

This is my favorite type of brainburner: Your next action is never straightforward, but not because you're overwhelmed with possibilities. Even tiles that grow the size of your meeple pool, which would seem to always be a good move, will give your opponents the advantage of time if played early. Yet if you don't grow your pool early, you may not have the meeples necessary to compete later.

Easily my favorite game and easily worth a 10. I don't see either of those changing any time soon.

RATING: 8
Using workers for bidding and activation is a really fun mechanic. The bidding mechanic and hidden information adds more player interaction which is usually lacking in worker placement games (e.g. "what!?! you still have yellow workers??"). The fact that not all tiles are used in every game adds a lot of replay value. I find the gameplay and iconography very intuitive. The overall game is pleasing to the eye; the artwork really hits the spot. I feel that not having all the information available (e.g. future season tiles, opponents' workers) cuts down on AP as well.

RATING: 8
Breese and co. does it again with their latest addition to the Key family of games. Keyflower is a bidding / city building game. It doesn't really do anything new, but manages to make old mechanics feel fresh with a spunky bidding system and beautiful tile set. This is your standard, "buy resources and trade those resources for better resources and cash them in for VPs to win" sort of game, but it doesn't come off nearly as plodding as Village does, for example. It actually feels like an Agricola/Le Havre hybrid, which are two of my favourite games and so it's no wonder I like this one. Less complex than those two, so this one definitely has its place. I am most appreciative of the random tile draw and how they are organized by 'seasons'.  There are certainly a few things about it that make it a bit rough around the edges - little extra rules and exceptions to make the game work mechanically but feel a little gamey.  Overall though, I'm impressed with the quality of this game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.9
Keyflower is a very pleasant and engaging Eurogame with an original mix of mechanics and an incredible amount of variability. The designation "worker placement" for Keyflower is quite misleading: a more accurate description would be instead that of an economic, bidding game where you use your workers as a currency, giving them away for activating tiles or for acquiring tiles and adding them to your own village. It also incorporates resource management, tile laying, pick up and delivery, set collection, open and secret objectives. It is also a very stylish "point salad" game, but not feeling in any way disjointed (like instead the majority of Stefan Feld's games do feel).

The experience is deep and satisfying. That said, Keyflower is not particularly suitable for occasional players. The core mechanic is quite easy to explain. It isn't a rules-heavy game, as much as an exceptions-heavy one! Also, getting people to get accustomed with the strategic nuances of the game could prove very complicated, if not frustrating, and the first game will always be a learning one for newcomers. Those who don't have the patience to learn, will not feel compelled to invest their time in this game. 

Luck is present in Keyflower, both in the form of randomly dealt workers and of what tiles come out each game. Nevertheless, it never feels luck driven, because players have a great deal of options to choose from to adapt their strategy to the layout they are confronted with.

The game is structured in a way that it can easily accommodate 2 to 6 players. Scalability is almost perfect, although with 5-6 players game time increases a lot and usually exceeds the 2 hours mark.

I recommend Keyflower to every serious Eurogamer, as a fresh and long-lasting experience.

We liked the game but felt it hard to control since there were several tiles on the table and was tough for us to pay attention to every player village and use his tiles to place our workers. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
In Keyflower, players bid on tiles to add their abilities to each player's settlements. What makes Keyflower unique is each player's settlement/engine can be used by anyone at the table. Filled with simple and interesting bidding and placement mechanics, Keyflower feels like a classic tableau builder efficiency game, but is more forgiving than modern titles. Allowing anyone to set a "trump" color and use any location opens up actions in a liberating way. There are several ways to be able to do the action you need. Some at the detriment of others and some purely beneficial to yourself. "Locking out" others from locations feels tactical, not malicious. Newer games do many similar things, but Keyflower has the pleasant strategy of an early-generation hobby game--one that is from farther back than 2012. The game also appears to scale well at different player counts.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7

RATING: 9
[b][Kim Williams' review of the game] found the rules surprisingly liberating.[/b]

:meeple: “Can I place workers on buildings in other people's villages?”
:) “Yes”

:meeple: “What about on the buildings which are being bid on?”
:) “Yes!”

:meeple: “Can I use a building that's already got someone else's Keyple on?” 
:) “Yes!!”

:meeple: “Can I add to a bid after I've been Outbid?”
:) “Yes.” 

:meeple: “Can I still add to my bid if I've not been outbid?”
:) “Yes!” 

:meeple: “Can I move and then use my outbid Keyples?”
:) “Yes!!”

:meeple: “Can I pass on one turn, and then decide to take an action on a future turn?”
:) “Yes”

:meeple: “Can I keep on taking turns even when everyone else has passed?” 
:) “Yes!”

RATING: 9
Looks like the great Richard Breese is an even better game developper or co-designer; after the fantastic Key Market, this game does not disappoint.  This is design by simplicity at its best: there is absolutely nothing superfluous in that box, yet each decision is an hard one.
Despite a painless rulebook, this is definitely not a gateway or family game - don't let the wondrous, full of light, artwork fool you: this is a gamers game at its best, where the complexity does'nt arise from needlessly convoluted rules, but from the depth of play.
I have one regret though - the graphic design; the icons are too obtrusive and prevent me to enjoy the charming artwork fully.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Keyflower is a game that is more than the sum of its parts - this is largely due to the fact that even though the game combines bidding, escalating worker placement, resource management and logistics, tile placement, as well as multiple currencies, the game somehow makes the parts flow into one another in a relatively seamless way. Meeples are almost magic in this game: they are currency, they are workers, and sometimes they are the currency to work on other's tiles. When they are also limited by a requirement to "follow the suit played" (regardless of the type of action they are used for), meeples become functionally multi-purpose in the same way cards are in San Juan, Glory to Rome, or Race for the Galaxy, or in the same way dice are multi-purpose in Quantum.

Other things this game has going for it is its refusal of outright economic snowballing and, accordingly, gives players reasons to continue to fight over tiles, resources, and privileges -- this game encourages some specialization, but paths are not so divergent and non-confrontational that you can coast to victory on a dominant strategy. Rather, there are many instances of self-benefiting moves which also hamper opponents plans. However, they are also likely putting you in a difficult bind.

It doesn't hurt that, like other Key-series games, the art is by Juliet Breese and, accordingly, the game looks great and is full of rich, pleasing details while remaining greatly functional. 

If I have a critique, and it is a small one, it is that the iconography is not always obvious at first.

RATING: 9
Keyflower is a Euro game which allows for the perfect amount of interaction between players! It caters to players who prefer to strategize/maximize and to brutal savages who love to straight up prevent all things that make their fellow player happy. You are forced to walk a fine line between barely scrapping together a functioning village and giving your opponents resources for their equally enticing village. The very workers (meeples) you use for performing actions also act as the driving economy behind Keyflower; this mechanic is so simple yet so ingenious. It really makes your decisions have weight, as your opponents can gain just as much as you have based on your actions. Keyflower is one of the most tightly designed games I've had the honor of owning/playing, and I consider it one of the best Euro games to date.

RATING: 10
Keyflower has one of the best blends of mechanisms, and somehow for once, the Euro settlement theme actually kind of feels right. The bidding, the worker placement, the resource collection (and conversion), the secret/public information, the winter tile choice(s), and the interaction are all absolutely brilliant. This isn't another straightforward resource conversion game. This game is also surprisingly tactical. Strategy matters, but the best players will be able to throw it out the window at the right time. I only see "luck" in the traditional sense mattering much for groups who don't look around and discuss game conditions often (ie, a player running away with an obvious strategy that had the right tiles come into play). Seeing combos come together (or missing a great combo by a small margin) is great. The game is tight with a lot of options available that diminish quickly. The interaction is both in your face and subtle, and it is absolutely critical. Sometimes it's easy to get caught up in your own tableau (or it's hard to be bothered to squint across the table). The replayability, especially with the expansion, is very high. I don't think I'll turn down a game of Keyflower for some time.

RATING: 7

In spite of the heaviness there's still a substantial random factor, when bidding for early tiles you don't know which late tiles will come out so there's no sense of relative value, you're just bidding and hoping. It's also completely themeless. Overall a typical Richard Breese design, all intellect, no soul.

RATING: 7.3
Mi primer juego de la serie Key. 

Consiste en un juego de colocación de trabajadores, subastas y cierto componente espacial de puzzle con mecánica "pick and deliver".

Durante 4 turnos, salen a subasta unas losetas. La moneda para comprarlas son los meeples (colonos que llegan al nuevo mundo a bordo de barcos) Los jugadores pujan por quedárselas teniendo presente que:

- Hay Meeples en tres (+1 extra) colores pero no se asocian con ningún jugador. Son colonos de "nacionalidades" distintas que no se llevan bien entre sí.

- Durante su turno, un jugador usa uno o más de sus meeples para pujar por una loseta (si gana, la añade a su poblado), o bien los usa para activar la loseta y hacer su habilidad (sea propia, ajena o ¡incluso en proceso de subasta!)

- Una vez que alguien coloca un meeple en una loseta (como puja o para activarla), el resto de meeples que se colocan en ella en ese turno, deben ser del mismo color.

Con esta mecánica, entre todos los jugadores van construyendo un "mecanismo" de obtención de PV´s y recursos. Lo que el jugador debe determinar es qué partes de ese mecanismo quiere controlar (para ello lo de pujar por las losetas) y qué partes debería explotar (situando sobre las losetas de los demás sus trabajadores, al estilo de Le Havre y sus edificios)

PROS: La mecánica de "subastas con colores" que cierra las opciones a los demás por no tener de tu color. Eso le hace ser uno de los mejores en mi colección. Funciona de 2-6 jugadores (teóricamente). Muchas opciones abiertas al poder usar las losetas de los demás.

CONS: Las losetas del último turno tienen algunas que parecen muy descompensadoras. La parte espacial y de pick and deliver parece añadir poco a la mecánica principal, tan sólo "duración".

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Superb medium-heavy thinky Euro.  The game has a lot going on, but it all comes together elegantly in a smooth composition.  There are only 4 rounds of play, corresponding to seasons in the year.  Players must choose from a tasty buffet of decisions with the goal of maximization of the final score.  The two primary mechanics in Keyflower are placing workers on tiles to perform work, which generates resources or placing workers in tense bidding activity to compete for the privilege of taking ownership of offered tiles that can be added to his village.

RATING: 8
Comments:
This may be true of all games, but here goes. In my opinion, Keyflower is, above all, a test to see how well you can assess game states. It tosses a wall of information at you and asks you to make good decisions based off that information.

Superficially, there is all sorts of other systems at play. You have an auction mechanic that is based around effective valuation of different tiles. You have a worker placement game that is slightly modified from that of an Agricola or a Le Havre by making sharing a possibility. In the end though, all the decisions you make in those subsystems are driven by how well you can parse the current game state.

Having played it a few times now. I feel like I've had my "click" moment where I start to realize how to play it reasonably well. Still, I'd like to sink some more time into it. Not least because I have two unplayed expansions, but also because I want to see if my assessment of its focus on assessment holds up.

No sleeves needed.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.7
[b]2 Player Review:[/b] 

This game was my first true love when getting into the hobby and remains on top even now because of it`s quick play time, the intensity of the bidding system and the puzzle of resource management. It is very tight, the hidden goals give you something different to aim for each game and you have to pay close attention to your partner`s actions to make sure they do not get a resource engine in place to outscore you at the end of the game. Have played it with 4/5 players and it works really well with all play counts, though the time increases obviously. 

[b]Both Expansions Review:[/b] 

I was greatly disappointed with [i]"The Farmers"[/i] expansion as a stand-alone expansion. The new animals mechanic is interesting, but having one animal per field is not worth the effort unless you have the Sheep Shelter which always results in a dozen sheep being piled on top of one another. Also adding the animal tiles to the mix greatly reduces the tightness of the game as it makes it very difficult to set up any kind of resource engine. If there were tiles to trade animals for resources I think the expansion would be a lot better. As is, you are often left with many buildings not upgraded because of the lack of resources, even with 2 gold for taking first place now. On top of all that, many tiles are often useless in Autumn because you do not have the resources to use the likes of the Forge, Blacksmith, Sawmill, Timber yard, etc. 
However, with all that said, once the [i]"The Farmers"[/i] is mixed with [i]"The Merchants"[/i] the game miraculously becomes fantastic once more! 
With the additions of the contracts, there is now always a valid use for all base game resources, even if the Autumn tile you're hoping for doesn't show up. And if you're left with a vastly useless autumn season, the extensions allow you to score some extra points from just upgrading tiles already in your town. I really enjoy the mechanic of locking colors allowed on a tile with the use of extensions as well. 
Overall, my hat goes off the Richard Breeze for turning a expansion I despised into something immensely enjoyable. 

[b]Pros:[/b]

- Set in the delightful world of [i]"Key"[/i]
- A nice variety of choices available on every turn
- Not knowing what colors your partner has always makes bidding tense.
- A lot of fun comes from balancing the use of your workers between bidding and activation of tiles. 
- Plays in around 40 minutes for 2 players.

[b]Cons:[/b]

- [i]"The Farmers"[/i] expansion
- You have to be very conscious of your partner`s actions as some of the resource engines can result in runaway scores.

Brilliant hybrid of action selection and auction. Workers = currency and actions. Bid on tiles with workers to build your town, then use workers to activate your buildings to generate points. RATING: 7.5
I found this to be a very good WP/auction euro, that should scale very well, has many ways to score, and turns generally move along at a good pace with a good game time to match. 

Some things I liked less than the above would be the usual meaningless theme, the colour WP restriction could be a little harsh at times (but usually you have other things you can do, just might not be what you wanted at times) and there was just a little bit of luck but it could sting a little at an inopportune time. It also had the Le Havre effect, where you need to look around the table to see if you wanted to use other people's buildings, only here it was much easier to see what they had. Oh, and I also don't like games with no tie-breaker unless there really isn't a good way to do it, not sure here but it seemed most tiles or workers could have sufficed. 

I should note that I played with the Farmers expansion which I rate an 8, it added to the game in a couple of nice (but small) ways including one other way to score and it also added a boatload more tiles. It also made going for the boats a little more interesting.

RATING: 10
Reigning all-time favorite.  Loads of replayability.  Scales better from 2-6 players than any other game.

Pretty interesting auction game.  Most of the game is bidding on tiles and then using them (yours and others') to gain points.  But you bid with meeples of one color rather than with money or points.  Meeples are the currency and once the auction has started for a particular tile, you MUST bid in that color.  If you don't have that color, too bad.  You can also bid for turn order.  

This is one of the hardest games I've ever played, and good meeple management is critical.  Lots and lots of brainburning fun.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
One of my favourite games. Its essentially an auction and worker placement game. But with this game there is an added twist, the workers you use for "worker placement" are also the currency you use to bid for the tiles. Players have to balance between using too many workers to bid vs using the tile's abilities. Another interesting aspect is the "gifting of workers" to other players just to use their tile's 
ability. Sometimes you're forced to do that in order to produce certain goods.

Only downside I can think of in this game is the fiddly setup at the beginning of the game since less players means some tiles had to be removed before the start of the game.

RATING: 8.5
Excellent game, on top of the usual worker placement resource game, there is also a lot more emphasis on figuring out what other players are doing. 

For example, you need to figure out what bid to put in on tiles that you think will last, and you also need a backup plan if you get outbid. It is possible to get lulled into a false sense of security if all other players are passing, only for one of said players to snipe your bid after you have played all your pieces (in this game the round doesn't end until all players pass consecutively, until then a player who has passed can still take a turn when play next comes round to that player).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Keyflower is interesting.  You are getting a handful of figures of different colors that are kept secret behind your screen.  You then in essence bid for the use of different tiles, with each bid beyond the first needing to be with figures of the same color.  You may get to add these tiles to your own set (I forget the mechanism).  You can still use tiles that have been claimed by other players but then they get to use the figures so owning a tile is a significant benefit.  Each tile does something different, so you can set up a machine where a tile creates raw materials that can then get processed into better materials and thence into points or figures for the next round.  So this is a low-key bidding game plus worker placement plus machine building.  It's got some interesting strategies to it.  I'm less enthusiastic about it than I might have been I think since it's mechanics are rather abstract and thus a bit disconnected from theme and I really like theme.  There's no story here.  I also didn't come away with the desire to play again to work out strategies as it is very tactical.  But otherwise I'd be okay with playing it, just not my first choice.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Keyflower has some very interesting mechanisms. For instance: I enjoy the way tiles can be used or bid on to acquire, the way each meeple color is like a different currency that changes what can be used to use or bid on a tile, and the hidden knowledge of tiles that can be used in the final round.

My problem is that the whole affair is a bit too chaotic. It can be difficult obtain the meeples you want without sacrificing other point scoring opportunities. Where players sit in relation to the start player can cause imbalances, especially in larger games. While the game does have sort of a reward for others using your tiles, it can be disheartening to have others use what you invested in before you can.

Keyflower reminds me of Ra, another game with auctions in which it is hard to control the bidding currency. I don't usually care for games like that, and while there is much I admire about Keyflower, it's not a game that grabs me with its strategic possibilities as much as it makes me dread being unfortunate.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
It's honestly the most "natural" feeling game I've ever played. It's like comparing football/soccer to baseball. Yes they both make sense once you've been exposed to them long enough, but the base mechanics of football/soccer just feel more seamless than baseball (not that this makes one game better than the other) . I find it crazy how poorly the Keyflower rulebook explains the rules given how intuitive the gameplay actually is.

It's amazing how versatile the game can is when there only 12 possible tiles that can flip during each of the 4 seasons, and they are all basically cycles (variations of the same thing for different resource types).

Last thing I love about it is that once you are playing with somewhat experienced players (5-10+ plays) you are basically forced to diversify or risk getting blocked entirely and scoring next to nothing. Some strategies when unencumbered will always score more than others, but that's mitigated by what tiles actually flip and how heavily you are forced to fight for the tiles you want.

The closest thing there is to a "winning strategy" is when the other players simply let you play solitaire which, again, will almost never happen with more experienced players.

I've played the game without any expansions easily over 50 times, probably closer to 100 and I don't see myself getting bored of it any time soon.

RATING: 7.5
2013-12-02

Initial Rating: 7.5 (November 2013)

Brilliant game, I love it. Keyflower has the potential to go even higher in my personal ratings. This is a great game, with simple rules but with very hard decisions (where to play/bid, which color should I play, should I play in another´s player village?)  At the beginning, the game is relatively easy and you have few options but as the game develops you begin to have a wider array of options. It can paralyze some gamers, so I want to play this with fast players only, also I prefer to play it with 4 players, more than that and the downtime can be an issue. It has some luck, but I feel I can react to it so it doesn’t really bothers me. 

The game components are high quality, and the art is very good too, most tiles` art clearly shows its function. 

The game runs for four seasons, still you feel that is way too short and you wish the game length was a little longer :D.

Great game, I want to buy it and I’m now official a fan of Mr. Breese (I also love Reef Encounter). I hope to play this soon again. It has a lot of depth but it is very simple rules wise.

RATING: 8
Keyflower is a medium-weight auction-based city-building game divided into four auction rounds (or "seasons"). Meeples are used for currency, which makes it stand out from any other game I own. Meeples come in 4 colors and when a tile is bid upon or used, only that color can used on that tile for the rest of the season. Most of the interest involves how to use the colors to your advantage to block or over-bid other players in the chosen colors. Turn order is very important to ensure you get first choice on the colors used on the tiles you need to use or win. Tiles you won in a previous turn are added to your village and meeples used to activate them are returned to you at the end of the season. You can also use tiles in other players' villages, but they'll get the meeples you put there. Actions used on tiles not-yet-won get removed.

Since most points come from point-scoring tiles in the final phase of the game, it is difficult to be sure if what you are doing will pay off as much as you hope. It is a bit of a learning curve to figure out how to position yourself in that uncertainty. The uncertainty brings high-replayability, but could also leave more strategic gamers feeling a bit empty. There are enough manipulation points to keep the game interesting for me and I'd be happy to play this anytime.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Like a euro cover song (auctions, logistics, worker placement, engine building, resource conversion, tile placement etc) but with a unique arrangement via its worker currency that helps it stand out. At its core is a four-round auction where workers act as tile activators, currency, and in some cases VPs. The resulting play provides an interesting sequencing puzzle intertwined with worker (currency) re-allocation. 

I acquierd this mainly with my wife in mind who longed for euros like Puerto Rico, Princes of Florence, Reef Encounter, and Cavum. This seemed similar enough whilst providing a unique experience in its own right. A little lighter and friendlier than I had imagined but she was pleased.

RATING: 7
I found the first game a little tough with so many different options, but it became clearer by the end. Everyone started with two possible victory tiles. This seemed problematic since these could drive your strategy, but these were not necessarily balanced between players. Plus everyone could bid on these at the end potentially ruining your strategy. The bidding mechanic was interesting of having different colored meeples that would lock in the color of a tile. The problem was you might really want a tile, but the colors you got were random so you might not even have a chance to bid. Building your own city in which you could then get back your meeples or those of other players was interesting. Overall the game seemed chaotic with lots of options requiring you to constantly adjust to what was available. This was also one of the least thematic Euros that I have played. The art and pieces were not that interesting. I would like to play it some more, but I am a little surprised it is rated so high on the Geek. I am guessing some of its appeal may be because it is so much less predictable than many Euros.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
After 5 plays one thing does concern me about Keyflower. The Winter tiles. It seems that to win you must successfully abuse one of the Winter tiles you are dealt. This often requires certain other tiles to appear in the game. This is bad for several reasons. First you might get dealt initial Winter tiles which are just bad, they do not all have the same scoring potential. Secondly you may be screwed because the other tiles you need to make a scoring tile work don't show up. 

The only solution to this problem that I can think of is to have all the Winter tiles face up at the start but this changes the game significantly. Perhaps more plays will show me this isn't a problem, but for now I'm very uneasy about this.

--------------------------------------------------------
--------------------------------------------------------

This is an excellent game! It mixes worker placement and auction into one. You can place workers to activate your own or someone elses tiles. You can even activate the tiles that are up for auction that round. The same keyples (a.k.a. meeples/workers) that you use to activate tiles also allow you to bid for tiles and if you win not only do you get to add the tile to your village you also collect any keyples that were used to activate the tile that round. Likewise, if someone uses your village tiles you collect their keyples to use next round. The keyples spent on winning bids are lost to the bag...

Bag you say? Yes, there are essentially 3 colours of keyples red, blue and yellow. You draw a random set of 8 at the start of the game and you get more between rounds when you win auctions for boats (new settlers arriving in the keydom). If you use a tile or bid on a tile you are choosing which colour of keyple will be associated with that tile for the duration of the round. If someone else wants to activate or bid on the tile they have to use the same colour of keyple. 

These mechanics are very cool, they present very novel and interesting decisions to think over. The game also has a lot of variety since only some tiles are used each game.

RATING: 7
On third play I didn't get screwed by the boat as we played it differently and probably correct for the first time, that the principal bidding colour remains the original colour unless all workers of the initial colour are removed. Otherwise, it would have happened the third time. Upgraded to 7/10 with this "new" rule.
----
Second play was a disappointment as well. One player targeted me in the last round repeatedly although she could have targetted anybody else as well. It cost me more than a fourth of my total points relegating me to a distant fifth place. As in the first play, the central culprit causing my dissatisfaction was the boat which allows a player to overbid in a different colour which knocked off my preparations against being overbid. It opens up random screwing (too) much. A pity since I started to enjoy the bidding and planning up to this point.
----
Keyflower works perfectly fine, has strategic depth and well thought-out new elements like using workers as currency for auctions as well as for activating buildings. It's interesting that workers can change loyalty when they are placed in opponents' buildings thereby increasing opponents' future action potential.
Still, my first play left me lukewarm. It might be the subtle but directed player interactions that can decide the game against you or the amateurish functional elements that nullify an otherwise exceptional artwork. Barring Reef Encounter, Breese's games always cause this reaction with me for some reasons. Although Keyflower got closest to convincing me and might rise to a 7 with more plays. Contrary to Key Market, it at least has an ease of play and an acceptable playtime once players got the rules down that makes further play more viable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Perhaps most reminiscent of Le Havre with a somewhat shoehorned spatial element, more randomness and lots of hidden-but-trackable information. There are ten resources divided into three types, which are of course employed and exchanged in various ways to produce victory points in the end. The key distinguishing elements are how you concurrently bid for and activate production and conversion abilities, and how this locks the currency usable for a tile on a turn. In the end you must realize much of your scoring potential by securing point generators in the auction, and slight differences in intent, bidding power and guesswork can lead to big point swings. The game is seemingly sensitive to mistakes, mischief and misfortune when it comes to competing for the same payoffs. Player screens are tricky to assemble and fit back in the box, but the rest of the stuff is nice.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Another game with a lot of interesting decision making. I love the worker placement aspect of the game. Anyone can use any building so long as they pay the proper amount of workers. The player who owns the workers get to keep them so it's beneficial to fight and try to win a useful tile since other players will most probably pay you to use it.
I also love how the colors of the meeples come into play. The color of the meeple determine the color of all subsequent meeple placement on that tile/bid. So a player can control/block a tile from usage/bid if he/she uses a meeple color which he/she is strong at.

Unfortunately, the end game mechanics using the winter tiles is not working out too well. A player may invest heavily on a type of play and yet might not be able to benefit from it if he/she fail to win the tile at the end of the game. This game is also fairly prone to analysis paralysis. Game duration might take longer than usual especially in a game where players have many meeples. 

I also don't really like how weak the spring tiles are in comparison to the summer and autumn tiles. It often seems that the game finally starts to run properly when gameplay reaches autumn but by then there's only autumn and winter left to play for. 

Despite all that, I still very much enjoy the game. Keyflower is one of my go-to game if I want to have something mildly heavy which keeps me fully occupied for at least 2 hours.

RATING: 7.5
One of my favourite games because of the unique mix of worker placement and auction. You bid with your available workers so you are using up your potential actions in order to acquire tiles into your tableau. The auctions also are dependent on the colour of the worker which adds to the dilemma of how many workers to bid when you might not have many workers of that particular colour. The auctions are cutthroat and lead to some very tough decisions on how many workers to sacrifice just to acquire a tile you really need. Mix those tense auctions with a unique tableau builder and worker placement game and this is a top 10 game for me.

Great worker placement game with a bidding element that is very engaging, the available options change every game and really keep the game fresh and tactical to a degree. I love the idea of using tiles in other player villages and all the strategic avenues the game provides.

RATING: 8.5
Mechanics-wise there's a lot going on, from bidding for buildings to add to your village (Homesteaders anyone?), the spatial element, worker placement, and resources requiring logistics (a bit of Neuland here perhaps). Despite all this the game doesn't feel needlessly complex or bloated, and the elements fuse together nicely creating a relatively quick game, considering the amount of things available to do.

The bidding is done using the different colors of workers as currencies. That and using others' buildings with your workers (but losing them to the buildings' owners after that) adds some needed interaction, without which the game would probably end up being a quite dry optimization exercise.

RATING: N/A
Almost like a resource/bidding/engine building Carcazzonne. Lots of strategy... 

Keyflower is a game for two to six players played over four rounds. Each round represents a season: spring, summer, autumn, and finally winter. Each player starts the game with a "home" tile and an initial team of eight workers, each of which is colored red, yellow, or blue. Workers of matching colors are used by the players to bid for tiles to add to their villages. Matching workers may alternatively be used to generate resources, skills and additional workers, not only from the player's own tiles, but also from the tiles in the other players' villages and from the new tiles being auctioned.

RATING: 7.8
The latest Key... game has some of the key features of the series (producing and movement on different landscape tiles, novel ways of worker placement etc.) and is a very up-to-date mix of trendy Euro games that try to re-invent worker placement. Actually, the core mechanism mixes two of my favorite lighter-middleweight family games from the past few years, Asara (worker placement with colored workers - the first one placed at a location tells you the suit that must be followed) and Lancaster (majority meets wp; defeated workers can be used again) with the also trendy "building your own display but having the possibility to use the other players' buildings" idea. And it's not even a very long game! It might be more complex and a bit fiddlier than what I really like but I rather enjoyed this title.

RATING: 9

If you see someone collecting skills, you better block them because that 10 points for every set of three unique skills is pretty dangerous (IE, I've scored 60 points off that tile already).

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I liked the little laughs and groans when outbid: this is nice, for a euro. This is a form of constant "combat" which is rarely present in most euros.

Also, I appreciated the mix of mechanisms (meaningful bidding, activation, currency (meeple) management, resources and their transport and use in upgrades, map creation,… The different powers are varied enough, and they are interlocking, so that even a small set of tiles lead to an exponential tree of moves (sequence of moves? color of workers? response of the opponent? bidding or activation?…).

My opponent found the game a little "diluted". I guess that thinking during my turns would have helped (instead of reminding me so often that it was my turn: looking at the tiles could have prompted new ideas, given new hints).

Probably not as tense as Caylus, for me (IIRC, I played Caylus once, and played Caylus Magna Carta many times).

The components are very good: nicely cut and painted meeples (the yellow paint could be thicker, on some meeples), thick enough cardboard, thick wood, nicely cut, nice drawings on the tiles,…

Easily forgotten rules:
- Outbid workers can be used for bidding, production/action, or bag exchange (Tavern, Fair, Peddler, Store,…, in which case extraneous workers are just lost to the bag).
- Tavern: the original worker is put in the bag *after drawing from it* (it is not a payment).
- The tiles on which we bid can be activated.

After playing Keyflower in the middle of other games:

I am not a huge fan of games where one gets points little by little, from many areas in parallel, if an optimization of many local options. I prefer games where my strategy is more focused at each moment in time, even if the strategy can shift (Clockwork Wars, but also Race for the Galaxy, and Puzzle Strike), as this gives them more flavor, by creating more of a story, or at list something that can be explained in relatively simple term: this creates clarity—where Keyflower leaves me wondering why in the world I lost precisely.

Still a very good Euro, I think, that has the rare virtue of having bidding that works with 2.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Mechanically this game is a masterpiece. Like Concordia it throws a smattering of mechanics together, but manages to make them do so seamlessly. All of the difficult decisions of a Euro but with tons of interaction via sniping of actions or bids. Like Power Grid, the interaction level is very high for a Euro, but you can still basically generally do almost anything if you are willing to pay through the nose for it. There are many things to consider; which tiles to bid on, which tiles to take actions, which tiles to upgrade, incentivizing your opponents to leach off your tiles so you can take their meeples, timing of everything, spatial aspect of your tiles and orientation, and turn order, with valuations changing throughout the course of the season as people make things more expensive. You also have to decide with what color and with how much you are willing to pay for everything to disincentivize your opponents. At the same time though the basic core concepts are very simple: place workers to bid for tiles or take actions, and so while there is a very high level of strategy, it is also not information overload the way that e.g. Eclipse or Terra Mystica are. 

The downsides of this game are that it is fairly abstract, and I'm really not a fan of the washed out colors and lack of shading in the artwork. It's not even that functional as the lack of distinction in the tiles makes them kind of blend together unless you're focusing on them. Also, while for the first half of each season gameplay flows pretty smoothly, the latter half when it is just one person taking their actions and everyone else having to verbally reaffirm they are passing can be quite annoying, especially if someone actually can take an action and is just waiting for the right time to do so (because then they still spend time assessing while for the most part not doing anything). Finally, while I am sure the way different people play really affects the game, I wish you didn't use 2/3 of the tiles each game for a 4 player game, and fear replayability.

RATING: 8
[i]Keyflower[/i] takes the standard "bidding for a tile" auction mechanic and adds an additional twist of having to match the opening bid's currency (which are meeples of four different types).  Alternatively, one can use your turn to take an action on a tile, spending increasingly more meeples to do it.  All in all, it makes for some interesting decisions.

RATING: 9
My first heavier euro and I still love it. Surprisingly thematic, it makes the gameplay easier to learn, but the possible strategies and engine-building are deep enough to keep you coming back for more so you can do just a little bit better. Bravo Mr. Breese, if repeated plays hold the initial first impression, also at higher player counts, this one is would be one of my "games I would save from the house fire" type of games.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is, apparently, the perfect mirror image of the many, many games cluttering up the market with their beautiful graphics and copycat mechanics: Keyflower boasts an innovative twist on worker placement, garnishes it with good narrative arc and nice tension between tactical and strategical considerations, and then manages to camouflage this potentially great game with horrible graphic decisions.

This is not to disparage the – as usually – wonderful and lovingly detailed artwork by J.Breese, but rather to condemn the blocky, harsh and uninformative designs that were plastered over them. Apart from uglifying the game, they also obfuscate some crucial things, like which scoring tiles need their resources in place and which don’t.

Enough about the single drawback, however, since the good things appeared to be outstandingly good at first sight. The bidding and using of actions with your workers does not only make for delightful puzzles regarding the tiles that are freshly auctioned off, it also offers a lot of possibilities with attracting workers to your own village by upgrading tiles that are useful to everybody. Tying the four game rounds to the seasons of the year is something I love in Rune Wars and – duh – Seasons, and it seems to work just as well here.

While I can tell that Keyflower’s is, at least for the first game, definitely hamstrung by it’s appearance, I find it hard to say whether the shoddy cover hides a very good or a great game without further plays. One way or the other, I’ll probably hold off from buying it for now, hoping for a 2nd edition with cleaner layout.

RATING: 8
Pros:

RATING: 10
This is such a neat game. We bought a used copy as a shot in the dark and man are we glad we did. Interesting use and combination of mechanics combined with a wide variety of increasingly difficult decisions... yeah, this is a keeper. I generally don't feel you need a lot of worker-placement games, but this one is so different it deserves to be in the collection.

After further play I am impressed by the balance of this game. It's never possible to tell if you are in the lead or not -- nothing becomes clear until the final count, which is then exciting because you don't know what's going to happen. In addition, this game is smooth. You play for two hours and it feels like thirty minutes. It never drags, every decision feels important, and every play feels different -- sometimes your tiles are so different that it changes the way you think so much that it feels like you are playing an entirely different game, not just session. A great game.

RATING: 9
This is a really interesting one. Even though the rules aren't complex, they're not intuitive, so it takes a play just to figure out how everything works toward endgame scoring. 

Once you realize how it all works, there's a beautiful mix of engine/worker placement/tiles/auctions that has so much to engage you in various ways, that's a pleasure to play.

But then the end-game comes around, and the auctions can (should) turn vicious for the various scoring tiles, in a way that makes me anxious, but completely turned off my wife in her first two plays. 

So I'll probably end up trading it, even though she likes everything else about the game. Just don't see a way to change it that doesn't break the game.

And traded it away 2015-08 for Roll Through The Galaxy in Canada-Wide Math Trade (with some local luck)

RATING: 8
Rating based on 6 players.

An interesting game packed with loads of mechanisms. It's a little on the fiddly side for me, as there are loads of tiles, each doing different things, put out at different times, etc., and there are quite a lot of rules... but I liked the worker-placement-as-bids, and how workers used on opponents' spaces are gained by them. The problem of having secret goals is mitigated by how you need to buy those goal tiles to actually make use of them. I think I would prefer the game with fewer players, as that would cut down the number of options, making it less of a sandbox and more competitive. With 6, keeping track of other people's villages became an issue (even physically, as they were difficult to see).

RATING: 6.5
What I like:
- Having to decide between using your meeples for auctions vs using them on buildings
- Being able to use other opponents buildings, although that could have a side effect of locking them out, which I didn't like as much
- Varied buildings adds variety to each game

What I didn't like:
- Sometimes the combinations of tiles that come out throughout the game and the winter tiles you draw synergize really well, or really poorly.
- Being locked out of using your own building can be pretty devasting to a strategy that you've spent all game trying to execute.
- To score a lot of points, you need to go deep into specific strategies, so it's hard to adapt to a new strategy if your current strategy fails.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Acquired in trade for Cry Havoc 12/11/16 with upgraded resources, Farmers expansion, Emporium & Monument, and Key Celeste.

Great game that has a lot of tense decision points.  The multiple use of meeples for production and bidding, with differing meeple colors is interesting and makes competition for boats fierce.  Even at 2p, this isn't nearly as mean as some people make it out to be.  I really enjoy how the tiles in each season differ, with Spring (production), Summer (powers), and Autumn (points) all having distinct focuses.

It's also fun to watch your city grow.  I can see some definite AP problems, especially as player count increases, since you can use anyone's tiles.

+ Interesting bidding/production use of meeples
+ Quick, simple rules with deep gameplay
+ Nice tile art
o Rules are a bit overwhelming at first read, but gameplay becomes intuitive after the first two seasons
- Huge potential for AP as player count increases

RATING: 8
A really tightly designed game, which belies its cute and friendly looking graphic design.  A limited number of moves, so the cost of overbidding for particular tiles can be huge as it really limits what other options are available.  Works very well with three players, but I can see how more than 4 would become unmanagable.

Some decent interaction available via blocking to inflate the cost of others taking those actions, bidding over the top to force opponents to move their meeples.  Fun and intense to play, but for gamers only though.  Don’t need to own a copy.

RATING: 5
Update: Trending down, just a bit too much that detracts from what I find the 'core' game - aka the bidding of meeples - also, it lost something with perfectly open info in a two player environment (which was to be expected on reflection), anyway - just down one point until it gets more play (and then proves itself or trends further down for me)

The balancing act of bidding and using your workers to generate resources (and even then - in your own village, or over there in you opponent's?) is the heart of the game. The rest is just trying to optimise for your chosen strategy - be it skill tiles, green workers or whatever - while trying to stay somewhat flexible in case you get screwed on your bids come autumn and winter. And last, some minor effort into how you want to build your village, a logistical puzzle that doesn't feel all that relevant to the game - just somewhat tacked on. It is these two latter things that I find less interesting, and keep the game from a higher rating, but the mean battle of worker placement / bidding for tiles is so much fun. I'd want more interesting and opaque effects on the tiles, or even more streamlined simpler to parse effects instead of this middle ground.

It really works along the full range of suggested players (2-6), not a common feature in games and for me a great plus. Also, it's easy to grasp, but doesn't get stale that fast due to the nature of auctions - valuations will shift as players learn the "ingredients" for certain strategies, which tiles are to be prioritised when going for so and so. And how to calculate the net effect of screwing someone from either getting a tile, or getting to use a tile they were counting on is an ever changing thing. I certainly don't see the point of expansions yet, though if it changes - well there are two, so that won't be a problem.

I'd like to play without hidden meeples, I think that'd make the best part of the game even more tense and interesting. 

A good game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Fascinating design where players use meeples of different colors as currency for bidding on tiles. The tiles are placed in your village and remaining meeples are used for worker placement amongst those tiles. 

Since you can activate tiles outside of your own village there are a ton of choices to make. When you activate a tile outside your village the owner gets to keep the meeples. Tiles can be reactivated but at the cost of paying more meeples. In bidding or tile-activation the color of the first meeple placed locks that color for the round.

Victory points are mostly achieved through cube conversions and there's an interesting pick up and deliver mechanism in order to move cubes to the right tiles. A lot going on but it all comes together nicely. You need to juggle a VP-producing village while keeping meeples of various suits.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Keyflower has such a great combination of mechanisms. In particular, the way workers are used for bidding and to take actions is just awesome. The game is loaded with wonderfully difficult decisions and some truly cutthroat player interaction (don't let the whimsical art fool you, this game can be downright mean). The expansions provide some nice variety and open up some interesting new strategic paths, but I need to explore them more. I particularly enjoy The Merchants expansion and would almost say that I wouldn't want to play without it now.

RATING: 4
Had this game been shorter or more interactive, it might have had a shot with me. There are some interesting mechanisms at work here, and the game components are attractive and high quality. All the signature Breese touches (draw random stuff and hide it behind your screen, glossary full of various tile effects, spatial element that's not central but can be quite significant, etc.) come together in a game that felt like a tightly designed Rosenberg game.

Unfortunately, I'm just not as interested in these games as the average BGG user is. Keyflower will undoubtedly continue to skyrocket up the rankings, and if you like stuff like Le Havre etc., then you'll probably appreciate this title. If on the other hand you run screaming from "spreadsheet" games in which you're converting resources and building up your little island/tableau, then you should stay far away. Me, I'd play it again--more than can be said of any Feld game--but I don't see myself requesting it.

UPDATE: ...And I did play it again. My opinion remains unchanged.

RATING: 8.2

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.75
The master of simple yet complex. 

The "stackable" worker placement used for production and bidding offer many varied options while keeping the game simple. Play consists of placing workers on tiles to do something (such as producing a good) or next to tiles to bid on them. With a successful bid you take the tile into your village, providing passive benefit and/or allowing you to produce on it and get your workers back. 

So many elements come together so cleanly it's crazy. The game is partially luck based, but very managable due to the bidding system. There are times when the appearance or lack of tiles can change the game, but almost all of the time there remain reasonable paths to victory. 

The game always seems to go very fast. Almost everyone I've played this with has said something along the lines of, "Oh, it's ending already?" I sometimes wish it went longer, but the reasonably short length (at least with 2-4 players, can't speak for 5 or 6) is often great.

The theme is quite minimalist but works well for me. The art is great and more theme would seem out of place in a game like this. Theme is sometimes necessary to make a game more understandable and smooth, but Keyflower manages that through its mechanisms.

A great, light-feeling but still very strategic Euro.

RATING: 6.9

Additionally, I can spend the whole game building up for my "Winter" tile only to have someone else steal it from me in the last minute.  This a weird game for me: a very random game which feels like it shouldn't be.

RATING: 7.5
An odd duck of a medium weight euro style game. Players have four rounds to establish property, secure resources, and deliver them to develop said properties into point-bearing upgraded versions all while auctioning to earn and use the benefits of these properties and even turn order with the same, non-renewing (but ever shifting) pool of workers that vary in compatibility based on color.

RATING: 10
Keyflower has been praised by many players and this is well deserved. This is a game where the different Euro mechanisms blend well together. Bid for tiles, produce from tiles, transport between tiles and upgrade tiles - all using the characteristic "keyples" of the game.

I'm usually not fond of games with many different tiles but in Keyflower, the functions of the tiles are easily understood and they are all known and available for bidding to all players.

But what I like most is the interesting interaction mechanism: you can use an opponent's tiles by giving up a keyple but also make it more expensive for the opponent to use the tile. This leads to a game where you're never out of actions (or opportunities to screw your opponents).

Reviewed at https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/1929535
Strategy at https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/1928936

*pas de max pour acheter la tuile, mais max 6 pour utiliser son pouvoir 
* Quand on produit des ressources dans son village elles restent sur la tuile de production, en revanche si les ressources proviennent d'un autre village ou du centre de la table, alors elles vont dans notre patrie

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
It has a semi-innovative and easy system as far as a bidding game goes. It's mostly about having to keep track and manage the timing and uses of your colored meeples. 

An interesting, kind of brainburning game that doesn't seem to drag forever. While rule is easy, game play is difficult, largely because you have to factor other players' buildings, which make the game better with 3 or 4 people.

The game is indeed kind of random. Because of the low amount of currencies (each players only get a few meeples and they have to bid with specific colors), most bids end up at 1, 2 or at most 3 meeples, positioning becomes huge. You also have to keep track of all the buildings and possible combination each player can utilize. There's far too much calculation out of your control.

This game is pretty neat as far as strategies go, but the sheer amount of randomness and huge amount of information needed to process for a simple bidding game may perhaps be too much for some people. I like it because it is thinky, but sometimes the luck factor can be too much.

RATING: 9
What a peculiar game. A first rules run through is not going to capture the beauty of this game.  On it's surface it appears to be a hodgepodge of mechanics.  There is bidding in an auction, there is worker placement / action selection, there is upgrading and resource conversion and engine building, there is spatial arrangement of tiles, there is movement of resources across tiles...  Hearing the rules, and you'll be like, "Oh dear, I guess I'll humor the owner with a single play."  A few minutes in, though, and man does this game sing.  Would never want to play with 2, but with 3 or 4, this might just be my favorite Euro of all time... need a few more plays.

The auction is brilliant.  The scope is limited, which I like, and the notion of establishing "trump" with color placement is extremely cool.  I also love how if a player is outbid, they can move they're bid to another tile.  But of course "bids" can also be workers that activate a tile's effects, and that also serves to establish "trump" for the tile.  So, you can also take that failed bid and plonk it down on a tile in order to activate it.  Brilliant.  And this is only one aspect of the game, because you are engaged in all of this activity in order to accrue resources for your own "village" (composed of tiles you've won), that you will need to carefully move around on your tiles, in order to upgrade them for VPs.  

Really, I can't do the game justice in a description.  This is one of those "the game is much better than the sum of its rules."  The iconography is very clear (for the most part), and the game duration is exactly right: about 1.5 hours, depending.  

My only beef, really, is the final scoring round.  The game is played across 4 "seasons." And different seasons have unique tiles that get put out for auction.  The winter tiles are ALL about victory points, and it produces a very swingy, mad scramble at the end of the game.  Reminds me a bit of Dominant Species where the end game feels quite a bit different from the strategic grind of the rest of the game.  All of your deliberations and care could come down to a somewhat lucky combo obtained by another player in the last round.  Personally, I find some "swingy-ness" kind of funny, but it does feel quite different from the rest of the game.

Edit:
Review that captures my feelings about the game.  Be sure to the read the discussion, as it is quite informative!
https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/909202/fantastically-fulfilling-meal-slightly-sour-aftert
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Over the four seasons in a year, players bid on tiles, or they may use tiles up for auction. The tiles are hexagonal, and players represent their bid with meeples placed along an edge of the tile. All tiles can be upgraded to earn more VPs, and tiles either give resources or allow you to transport goods or upgrade tiles. Boat tiles also give special abilities as well. Each season, one can also bid on turn order for next season, and you also bid for boats with meeples and skills. 

The trick with the bidding is once the color is set, further bids can only be of that color. As well, a fourth, rarer color meeple can also be gained as well, adding a layer of tactics to the game. 

There is a lot going on in this game, especially when you have to look around and see who has what tile, since you can use that tile by donating a meeple(s) to that person.

It's a decent game, with many things to think about.
