RATING: 9.4
Wow, great game. Looks beautiful. Nice feeling of constructing a village which we love. Clever use of meeples as workers as well as currency. Quick, simple. 

Although some people claim it could be a gateway, it's really little too complex I've found. It's fairly rules light - but there's a lot to take in every time a new set of tiles get put out, and new players can get hosed in that final round very easily which might leave a sour taste in their mouths.

We have houseruled the last round so that at least 2 of 3 the winter tiles players are holding come out. That way if someone loses their first choice they can have an okay second one. Otherwise that last round get VERY cutthroat and mean, if everyone just puts down one winter tile for example - quite out of character with the rest of the game.

*also own: Keyflower, the Farmers*

RATING: 4
Too many game aspects: worker placement, bidding, tile placement and match the colours. There are much better worker placement games, much better bidding games etc that I would rather play. I have never had any desire to combine several games and play them all at once. 

PROS:
-Using meeples for bidding and worker placement is a seemingly innovative and interesting aspect (but doesn't integrate that well). 

CONS:
-Winter tiles are hidden and victory point potential varies dramatically depending on how game setup proceeds. Some players have an extreme advantage right from the start (but its hidden). Others have little to work with. You could guess what others are doing and try to do the same or try to block them, but only one will win the relevant winter tile at the end. However, most likely both would lose the game.  
-If everything was open information, runaway leaders could be identified and dealt with better (thus more player interaction). 
-The ability to knowingly block someone in a worker placement game is a critical aspect and it is missing here. 
-If there was no bidding, it would be a solitaire game. Little to no player interaction outside the bidding. 
-Putting your meeple on another players tile is not promoted, game promotes solitaire activity. 
-Boat 4A lets someone break the rules of bidding, bringing in a differing coloured meeple to a specific colour bid (killing strategy).
-Multi use meeples seems counter intuitive; spend the meeples for bidding and now there are not enough to place out to gain resources. Balancing this is the strategy but is boring. Would like the game to be a bit more dynamic, include in game money etc.
-Worker placement where we need to bid on the tiles where we will put our meeples?
-Theme: using meeples for bidding represents slavery? Are we cashing in on a bounty?

Note: Farmers expansion adds yet another game aspect; Carcassonne with animal meeples.

RATING: 8
There are a lot of nice things in this game: workers of different colors, but once one starts to bid / use a tile with a color, everyone must continue using that color;  player can choose to bid or use a tile in their move; one can even use tiles which are still being bid for or belonging to other players, even if losing the workers in the process, etc. 

The game offers really a lot of interesting choices.

Replayability is huge: each game offers different challenges, possibly one of the most variable games I have ever played.

Small disadvantages for my tastes are that it may be a bit difficult to apply a consistent strategy (tactic counts!) and that there is a bit less control, also due to some luck: for example the way the winter tiles (i.e. the goals each player "knows" alone at the beginning of the game) and the other tiles that actually come into play may form combinations that are more advantageous for some players than others. Because of these disadvantages, I think the game is suitable also for the casual gamer.

But these are minor quibbles. I like this game a lot, and as many new games I have played recently, I have the feeling that we are at a new stage of boardgame design, new games are getting better and better... and as many of this new generation games, Keyflower manages to compress in quite a short time a lot of gaming experience.

UPDATE 1 Jan 2012
Lowered the rating from 9 to 8. The lack of control due to some luck and even more to the hidden information seems to be more than I expected and like. Also somehow the game is a bit less tense than other new "second generation" games I am playing in this period. The game is still very good and I still like to play it. Also its huge replayability is confirmed: every game seems a new one.

RATING: 9
Maybe the high rating is a bit premature but if anything 'Keyflower' could potentially even move up to a 10; that's how much I like it. Simply awesome how it integrates worker placement with bidding, resource engine and special building abilities. While the rules are not overly complicated for a game of this complexity the many choices you have during a turn and the various strategies you can employ give rise to a very challenging gameplay. It never bogs down, though, as turns are over quickly. Plus, it is high in interaction when players bid for the same tile or want to use the same one for production. Overall, I see myself enjoying this one for a long time!

RATING: 7
somehow I remembered this game as being more fussy than it is. While it isn't simple, Keyflower renders its complexity almost completely via its worker color-based auction mechanic, which takes up 75% of the game time-wise. Like the best auction games, the point of bidding is to benefit yourself but also to make the cost of others' strategies more prohibitive. What invariably happens is that the dynamics of area majority get applied in the auction; to wit, players gang up on the perceived-strongest/most aggressive players, and in the end, the perceived-weakest player often emerges as the surprise winner. So far not seeing what's Top 30 about Keyflower, but it's a good enough game.

RATING: 9.3
A pure delight. The charming artwork belies a ruthless auction and worker placement game with great tension and an enormous breadth of strategic possibilities. I am deeply impressed with the sheer variety of different winning boards that I have seen, as victory points can be had from the collection of tile upgrades, workers, skill tiles, or resources - but the viability of any given path is conditional on the appearance of certain endgame scoring tiles, with each player having partial foreknowledge of a different set of those tiles. The interlocking of the mechanisms in this design is stunning.

RATING: 10
[size=9][b][c]OVERALL REVIEW:     :star::star::star::star::star:[/c][/b][/size]
[size=8]The game has a clever use of the hexagonal tile faces during the auction phase, and path making during the village expansion phase. The hidden player assets and special green meeples keep the game tense, while players try to outmaneuver each other with bids and tile actions.
[color=green]My favorite route/network building game.[/color][/size]

[size=9][c]RULES SIMPLICITY:   :star::star::halfstar::nostar::nostar:[/c]
[size=8]Actions intuitive after a round, but tile abilities require rule consultation[/size]
[c]QUICK SETUP:        :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:[/c]
[size=8]Tiles need organizing by season, and adjusted based on player count[/size]
[c]SHORT GAME LENGTH:  :star::star::halfstar::nostar::nostar:[/c]
[size=8]Depending on players, ninety+ minutes[/size]
[c]MAXIMUM STRATEGY:   :star::star::star::star::star:[/c]
[size=8]Hidden "currency" makes for a lot of bluffing[/size]
[c]MINIMUM LUCK:       :star::star::star::nostar::nostar:[/c]
[size=8]Random tiles per season, but no so many to make strategies futile[/size]
[c]PLAYER INTERACTION: :star::star::star::star::star:[/c]
[size=8]Every auction bid and tile activity helps or hinders other players[/size]
[c]COMPONENT QUALITY:  :star::star::star::star::star:[/c]
[size=8]Thick tiles, lots of meeples, and cool player screens[/size]
[c]THEME INTEGRATION:  :star::star::star::star::star:[/c]
[size=8]You feel like settlers competing for resources and building a village[/size]
[c]REPLAYABILITY:      :star::star::star::star::star:[/c]
[size=8]Merchants expansion, and different tiles scale based on player count keep it fresh[/size]

[b][c]BEST WITH:[/c][/b]
[size=8]4 players[/size]

[b][c]MY PLAYS:[/c][/b]
[size=8]in-person with 3, 4,5 players[/size][/size]

[ImageID=1448755 small]

Keyflower combines several game mechanics within a cohesive theme. The auction phase is open for the entire duration of the round, where players can bid on tiles or alternatively activate the special ability of a tile in auction or a tile on [i]any[/i] village. Also in the auction phase, players are bidding on turn order, which includes order of selection for the incoming ships and free resources. After all players pass, the auction tiles are distributed and players expand their village. Any meeples used to activate an auction tile you won or activate a tile in your village, become your property. The addition of special green workers that must be purchased, creates an uncommon resource that can be used surgically against other players.

As the game progresses, the player villages become more and more important for resource management, and end game victory points.

[color=#3388cc][b]Pimp it?[/b][/color]

I suggest an opaque bag to store and pull the skill tiles. I believe the Merchants expansion comes with such a bag.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.8
Medium worker placement euro that features several original mechanisms.

The graphic design is beautiful, following the tradition of the Key series. The components are nice, with plenty of meeples and thick cardboard tiles.

El theme is there, but many times the mechanics are more important.

The good:
-> Lots of interaction, in all moments.
-> Many paths to victory. Many scoring possibilities.
-> Each action is a decission between plenty of possibilities.
-> The bidding, action and worker placement and recovery mechanics are just brilliant.

The bad: 
-> Rules are not as easy as they seem. In the first games, you'll find cases during the game where you'll want to check the manual. 

After reading the rules and the good reviews, I already expected something good, but it really surprised me how well the game works. You can try very different things and as long as you do well, you'll have chances to win.

I have only played once and the game dragged a bit because of the rules (I should have read the manual with more attention). I'm pretty sure I will increase my rating considerably with further plays.

RATING: 9
Un euro de formato multimecánicas (estilo Kramer o Friedemann) que me sorprendió gratamente por la profundidad que ofrece. Puede parecer apabullante en principio por la enorme cantidad de opciones, pero la mecánica general es bastante simple. Hay muchas cosas sucediendo al mismo tiempo y las interacciones tienen consecuencias complejas. El paso será tan fluido como la capacidad de procesar información de los jugadores. Es de los pocos juegos que me provocó análisis-parálisis, la acción en ronda paso a paso le otorga un factor táctico a la resolución que no podés dejar de tener en cuenta. Es interesante que las opciones no se basen puramente en el cálculo (estilo Power Grid), sino que se sientan como decisiones. En ese sentido, hay que jugarlo varias veces para lograr la necesaria coherencia en las estrategias y control sobre los resultados. Hay procesos que habrá que encadenar y un importante elemento de momento (timing) en la toma de decisiones ya que los demás jugadores pueden interferir en la cadena de tu proceso. Esa combinación de estrategia a largo plazo y ejecución táctica logra un juego desafiante, tenso e interesante a cada momento.

Si hay algo que me hace ruido es el encadenamiento de tiles estilo Carcassonne. No veo que le sume personalidad al diseño y agrega un scoring final un tanto arbitrario. Es como un micro-game solitario que no aporta mucho a la dinámica grupal de la partida. Sin embargo, el tomar-y-llevar que genera es una mecánica de peso en la engine. Suma complejidad sin ser complicado, pero es complejidad por la complejidad misma, y en ese punto, no se si más complejidad en el esquema de decisión era algo que el diseño necesitaba. De hecho, se juega mejor con 4 porque de a 6 la complejidad atenta contra la fluidez de la partida.

De cualquier modo, muy recomendable.

RATING: 8
心得:
機制真的頗特別 
要競標同時又可以放工人
又要拼板塊
感覺要思考層面頗多的
因為競標的關係
三人以上可能比較好玩
規則:
*起始拿屏幕，每人隨機抽八個工人，一個隨機家版塊(數字最小的拿紫色米寶成為起始玩家)
*根據人數放船板塊，上面抽對應的工人跟技能版塊(蓋著)放置，也根據人數放順位版塊
*每一季版塊根據人數使用(6-7-8-9-10),冬季版塊每人跟據人數拿(3-3-3-2-2)，冬季時每位玩家打出1塊以上的冬季版塊來競標
*每輪可做動作如下，直到pass為止(下次輪到還是可動作)，全部人一起pass則一季就過去
1.競標，放人在你的對應位置，競標人數要比之前多，顏色要一樣，輸的工人可整組拿去做其他事(可加人)，但不能收回屏幕後
2.放工人在競標版塊(顏色要一致，標到的人拿走)自己家園(結束可回收)或別人家園(結束送人)執行動作，一個建築可以執行三次每次工人數要加一，一個建築上最多六個工人
*自己家園生產的資源放在該格，其他生產的放在家版塊，升級需要的資源必須在該建築上
*季節結束，沒競標成功的收回，沒被標的版塊移除，成功競標的付出工人(放回袋中)，拿板塊去拼(版塊可旋轉)，收回板塊上工人，按標到的順位挑船上的東西，沒標順位的就從上回的起始玩家開始輪流拿(沒人標順位版塊起始玩家順時鐘換人)
*冬天時則把拿船跟順位版塊回去拼跟計分，一樣每人做多一個船板塊
*最後計分紫色起始玩家標記可以被當成任意資源工具或工人記分
*黃金沒被記分板塊計分的話一個一分，平常黃金可代替任意資源

大五月花號

RATING: 8
I haven't been too impressed with any of the Key-themed games - nothing that hit me as better than average. But I'm glad to say this is the first one that's struck me as really good. In essence it's a VP hunt using cube acquisition and VP conversion, but the manner in which it is performed is innovative enough to warrant interest and exploration. The tiles that are being bid for in the middle, each with a special power (usually to generate cubes, move cubes around, get more meeples, etc), can also be used even while they're being bid for. Tick. Tiles that are won are placed into your village at the end of the round, where you'll be able to use them for free ... but other players can also use them, which increase the cost to you to use it, but with the benefit of them donating meeples to you for your use next turn. And if someone has used a tile, you can also use it, if you pay a bit more. So although it's likely you'll use your own tiles, there's enough interactive nervousness about to get on your own tiles quickly if they're important for you. So there's another tick for the right amount of race tension - get on quick, but if you don't, there's other things you can do or you can still do it if you want to pay a bit more. There's a lot of options to consider, which has the ability to slow things down if you're playing with AP prone players, or those who are so focused on winning that they want to track the meeple counts of each player in and out (bleh). But played reasonably, the game ticks along fine and comes in at the right length for its weight. Interesting.

RATING: 6
What I like:
- Extremely clever mechanisms; innovative
- By far the most attractive Key game
- Pretty simple rules
- High replayability in terms of what tiles are in the game

What I don't like:
- Auctions are pretty cutthroat
- The ability to use others' village tiles can be nasty
- Luck of the draw can be cruel (worse than Thebes)
- Complex interactions result in real brain-burn
- Our 2p learning game took 110 minutes. Presumably it would speed up a fair bit.

This game was more or less what I expected. There are so many options, and you constantly have to prioritize both from a "which resources to use" standpoint and more importantly, "when?". 

I didn't realize how important resource generation was, in my first game. Grab those tiles in spring, because you'll never have another chance. Some of the ships seem powerful, too.

If you have a great memory, you'll have a big advantage in the game. It might be better (or at least fair) with open information.

The BGG-voted 3.3 weight is more based on depth; the rules themselves aren't that complex.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Keyflower combines the concepts of worker placement and bidding in a very interesting way.  Players use their pawns to both bid on tiles (by placing them next to the tile) or use them (by placing them on the tile), and whoever owns the tile gets to keep all of the pawns that used it for later turns.  Subsequent bids and uses of a given tile in a round require one more pawn than the last bid or use (respectively)--and all pawns on a tile must "follow suit" with the color of the first pawn played there.  There were also some tile-placement and pick-up-and-deliver elements once you owned a few tiles, but really the interesting part was the auction/tile use system.  I thought the game worked well together, and I was happy to see a mechanic that I hadn't seem in boardgaming before.

(3 plays)

RATING: 8
I'm expecting this game to become more and more enjoyable as time goes on. It has a lot of interlocking mechanisms that work really well (auction, WP, tile laying, pick up and deliver), but it's also very open in that way, the choice between bidding on or activating a tile is sometimes an excruciating one. Actually seen it induce high levels of AP in people who are generally very quick in taking turns. It's a bit hard to get your head around how to get points, how to use resources etc. until you see how the mechanisms work together, which takes a few plays. Also learning which types of tiles come out for spring, summer, fall and winter is helpful. Play with a group who likes a fair bit of "take that", it can get nasty when bidding or blocking someone from an action in their own village. When played with the right group, this game really shines.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very interesting and fun auction / worker placement game, with unfortunately a somewhat unsatisfying end game. Keyflower offers a lot of game in relatively simple rules and short playing time.

Keyflower has an ingenious system where the workers you place (on tiles, to gain resources) are also used to bid for tiles. Winning tiles is good, because the workers you place on tiles you own you get back for the following turn, so the only cost to doing that is an opportunity cost: you're not placing them somewhere else, such as in a bid for more tiles. While workers you place on other people's tiles you lose (and that person gains them), so there's a very real cost in doing that.

Another nice aspect is that only about 2/3rds of the tiles are used each game, which makes for a very different experience each game. One game there might not be many opportunities to gain wood, the next skill tokens will be scarce, and in a third there will be very little transportation. So you need to adapt and you can't place a fixed value on each resource.

What I also like is that you can't do everything; but you don't have to. You won't have a lot of tiles AND workers AND resources AND green keyples AND tokens AND transportation, but you can get away with almost anything. If halfway through the game you have only won one tile but you have 10 resources, you can still win, and if you have no resources at all but you do have a handful of green keyples you can also still win, &c. You just have to figure out when to use your opponents' tiles, what to bid on and so on.

This diversity comes with a downside though: the ending feels lacking. Keyflower is ostensibly about bidding for tiles, placing on tiles to gain resources, and using those resources to upgrade your tiles for victory points. And throughout most of the game that's what everybody's doing. But victory points for upgraded tiles will only account for about 1/3rd of your total vp. The rest comes from scoring tiles, and no two players will have the same tile. So player A might have a tile that gives her 2 points for each of her 9 green keyples, player B owns a tile that gives him 20 points for owning two of each skill token, and player C gets 21 points for moving 7 iron on a tile that gives 3 points for each iron there. Does this mean that player C played better than player B, who did better than A? It's hard to compare.

In rounds 3 and 4 players are mostly doing their own thing. Keyples, tokens or resources, they're all worth nothing at the end of the game, unless you have the correct scoring tile, in which case it's in your best interest to gain as many of that item as possible. So instead of competing for the same thing, every player is specializing.

I feel a game is better when player have multiple ways to reach the same goal, but in Keyflower players use the same method to reach very different goals.

If you're looking for a game you can play a lot, where you can try out different strategies, switch things up, play nice or very competitive, reactive or proactive, Keyflower delivers. If instead you're looking for a game that lets you hone your skills, develop a winning strategy, become more efficient and improve your scoring, Keyflower might not be the game for you.

[thread=909202]I decided to expand these initial thoughts into a full review.[/thread]

RATING: 10
Fantastic game that scales well no matter how many players you have. I love the tension of taking actions, bidding for tiles and trying to manage the flow of getting new workers all with the same pool of meeples. The mechanic where each tile can only be used by workers of a single color at a time is really interesting and innovative. I also get excited by the carcassonne-esque way tiles from the center get added to your personal town, whereupon other players can use them and you get the benefit of keeping the guys they spend. Really clever! All in all it's a mix of worker placement, bidding, tile placement and resource management.

Certain winter tiles can create point combos of unbeatable power. Players need to know about those tiles ahead of time and prevent players from exploiting them.

:star: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:star: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:star::star::star: Enjoyment

RATING: N/A
Played Once
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Pros:
* I've only played this once so far, but I can see how no two games would be the same. The secret is in having so many almost-game-breaking combos that so greatly impact your strategy, and in knowing ahead of time which of your secret almost-game-breaking scoring opportunities you're going to have to fight for in the end.
* Plays perfectly for 2 players.
* The auction mechanic is amazing and the tiles actually have measurable and relative value even on your first play through. The best part is the almost-game-breaking tiles are often only almost-game-breaking for one person or the other. When you combine this with you immediately getting your keyples back from a losing bid, it leads to fun spiteful moves where you force the price of something up knowing full well the other person will pay for it and you'll get your resources back. We had one situation where my opponent had a single blue meeple on about three tiles he really wanted. I put down two on his most desired tile, which forced him to go to three which let me move those two to his next most desired tile and we cascaded down the tiles until he'd spent most of his blues. He then had to use his meeple generator tiles to try to get more blues (turns out two of the tiles we were fighting for were the winter boat tiles which shouldn't have been up for bid, but the point is that there's room for a ton of really fun player interaction in this game).
* Great player interaction. See above spite-auction example.
* I love how your winter scoring tiles were kept a secret from the other players but you still have to bid for them in the end! We only had a single tile come up that allowed a player to get green meeples. It was so important to me to get my winter scoring tile that I had to use up three of my meeples on my first winter move to keep my opponent from generating greens.
* Just the right amount of randomness. Every turn created a new puzzle to solve but you never felt trapped by a bad draw. I lucked out and got an anvil on my last skill tile draw for 3 more Scholar points, but if it had been a saw instead I might have upgraded my Timber Yard or fought harder for the Scribes tile and if it had been a pick axe I might have spent it in my opponent's Brewer.
* From reading a few session reports and other comments, it sounds like winning scores are often in the 50 point range. With scoring tiles, point tiles, gold, etc maybe giving you on average of 5 points, it feels like every scoring opportunity matters but missing out on something won't knock you out of the game.

Cons:
* Nothing really stood out as a con. There were some things that might cause me not to play the game as often (length, complexity), but I can't blame a two hour, heavier game for taking two hours and being heavier. That said ...
* It's a longer game than I usually get a chance to play, especially with open information (which can greatly increase AP).
* Heavier and mathier than we normally play too.
* It's not a con for me because I have them, but there are some possibly hard to track down promo tiles for this game. Key Celeste, in particular, has a very unique mechanism that's worth trying. If you can't track down the tile, maybe use Boat 4b and a grey meeple from, for example, Carcassonne as a stand-in.

Neutral:
* I usually dislike games with player screens, but I'm putting this in the neutral section because playing with all trackable information and initial meeples out in the open doesn't break the game. Drawn skill tiles and winter tiles can be kept face down so no need for screens.
* Normally I dislike games that are AP inducing, and this one certainly counts, but it's AP inducing the way Battleline is; because of the huge impact a player's move can have on you, there's a quite a bit of tension for everyone involved while waiting for the other player to make a move. In a two player game, the impact of the other player isn't so great that you can't do some planning on your own turn.
* When it comes to handling points, I generally dislike games with dozens of tiny scoring events in a score-track-lapping point-soup meander around the board. My favorites are usually race games where the first person to achieve a victory condition wins. Keyflower sits in the middle. There's nothing to track during the game, but you have to add up a bunch of things at the end to see who won.
* No cards. Neither a pro nor a con, but as an obsessive sleever, I'm glad not to have to sleeve anything this time.

= 2016-01-25

I was (finally) able to play this game again, this time with my lovely and intelligent wife. She gave the game two chances and has declared it is not for her ("felt like work"). I enjoyed the game immensely, but I will admit it breaks down somewhat if people aren't willing to aggressively bid for tiles. This is not a game for nice people.

= 2014-03-02 - R:8

Wonderful game. THIS is how you mix game mechanisms without turning things into a jumbled mess!

I've only played it once, but my instant love for this game is making me wonder what characteristics elevate it from being just another vapid point-soup / spreadsheet optimization game into a brawling slug fest where every action matters. Or maybe that's it. The level of interaction is high - not just in the "you have to pay attention to what your opponent is doing" kind of way that the Euro apologists are always espousing, but actual I-can-not-believe-you-just-did-that-to-me interaction.

The game "only" lasts four rounds and when you complete the first round in under 10 minutes you'll wonder if you did something wrong. When your last three moves take a nail biting half an hour of back-and-forth aggressive maneuvering, you'll realize the only thing you did wrong was wait so long to play this game!

I admit the reason I picked this game off the shelf last night was to prove I disliked it so I could put it up for trade/auction while my expansions were still NiS. While the game didn't directly succeed in helping me reduce the size of my collection, this was one of the first times I've ever played a game and felt, at the end of it, that the replayability was high enough that I won't need another game to fill this niche for a long time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Nov 2016: Upgrade from 5 to 6

* Seems very playable from 2-6 players
* Much better than Key to the City - London.
* The skill tokens nor the road network mechanisms don't feel as vestigial as I thought. They are more intuitive and more fun than KtoC-L's connectors.
* Still, my comments below apply.

Oct. 2016: Lighter than I expected considering the number of mechanics packed into this game.

In my only play, I pursued the strategy that seems to work in a lot of Euros: Focus on strategies no one else is doing. Seemed to work very well in this title, which to me is a fault.

The game felt oddly scripted even for only one play. In each of the four phases, it seemed that players should focus on doing one thing. First phase is getting tiles, and second-to-last phase involves spending your remaining resources. I suppose, though, that any engine-building Euro goes through similar phases, but it felt too structured here.

The game offers a challenging puzzle to figure out how to get all the interlocking mechanisms work on your behalf, but then that means there's no time to get in the way of your opponents' plans; like many Euro's, getting in the way of an opponent is usually a side effect of pursuing your own goals.

I do agree with some comments that some of the mechanisms feel vestigial, such as the road network mechanism or the skills tokens. They feel more like distractions; I don't really care to take them into consideration when making more significant or important decisions, such as which tile I want from the common pool.

So, Keyflower succeeds as a Euro by posing challenging puzzles for players, albeit not very elegantly. Still, I'm not a big fan of Euros, and this title still felt too similar to others in the same genre. However, I'm looking forward to the sequel, Key to the City - London as I hope that game can serve as a stepping stone from lightweight games to heavier ones for "non-gamer gamers."

RATING: 8
Summary: Interest auction format with tile laying. The game progresses into a strategic cutthroat bidding game towards the very end. 

Pros:
1. Auction: The auction mechanic is great in this game. It's not a simple question of how much? It's also a question of which color to bid in. You can take a tile on the cheap if you can remember which colors other players are running low on.
2. Quick: For a solid euro game, it's a fast game. A 5 player game only takes 1.5 hours.
3. Tactical: This game is more about doing the most with what is directly in front of you. It's about burning your brain to optimize your moves.

Cons:
1. Strategy: This game lacks a long term strategy. You don't know what the majority of the scoring tiles are available until the end of the game. This makes it difficult to gear towards any specific long term goal.
2. Other Players: You have very little control of the board. You can get screwed out of the majority of the tiles just because all the other players have yellow meeples and you only have red. This can cause you to get almost totally locked out of a round. At lower player counts, it's less of an issue. At higher player counts, it can become horribly frustrating.
3. Family Feud: This game has a family feud effect. The first 3 rounds score you minimum points and the entire game revolves around the last round. You're really just accumulating as much as you can in the first 3 rounds and hoping for good scoring tiles in the last round. 

Summary:
This is a solid game. The auction/action system of different colored meeples makes the game worth it to potentially own. However, this game ends up feeling more strategic than it really is. I tend to not like auction games, but this game has a very forgiving auction system. I enjoyed the game overall. I'm likely to play it again, but I don't know if I'll run out and buy it.

RATING: 4
Have played twice, will try once or twice more ... maybe.

It's boring.  The endless reevaluation of bids and available spaces means that you cannot plan for your turn during the long downtime between turns.  Worse, you cannot joke with your table mates because it will distract the acting player.

The malfunction is inherent in the central mechanic of meeple management.  The value of meeples is unknown and inflation can rise quickly.  Since the same money pool is used in two different markets, it becomes quickly impossible to estimate their value to your opponents.  So you are just guessing, and probably guessing poorly.  This is compounded by the hidden scoring tiles.

This game is the cardboard equivalent of watching a coocoo clock with no birdie at the hour.  It is clearly a fine work of craftsmanship but fails to deliver the triumphant experiences I have come to expect in boardgames.

RATING: 7
2017-06-22: Initial imressions after two games (6-player & 2-player)
I don't know... I really would like to love this game, but I don't know.... The mechanic where you use meeples both for bidding & activating the different tiles is novel, and the availability of meeples is tight enough to ensure interaction throughout the four rounds. It is clear that you should build up an engine based on your winter tiles (which every player has some, but nobody can be sure, that they can aquire them at the end of the game), but somehow I miss the tension in the game. 
The idea that some random tiles are not in the game also could increase replayability, on the other hand it could screw you, if those were crutial for your goals/engine. 
I would like to play 4-5 more games before final judgement, but I have some concerns if this has a place in my collection. 
Oh, and the rulebook sucks, I don't know, why it is hard to put examples in every rulebook!!!! If you would like to learn the game I highly recommend the Watch it Played! video:

RATING: 9.85
Keyflower has an incredible mix of many mechanics that cretes a rich and deeply satifing game play. The game begins with a mix of worker placement and bidding but what is truly unique it that they are done simultaneously.  On top of that there us an area control battle for every tile used and added to the game as only one color of meeple can used on each tile (for worker placement or bidding). Once tiles are one they are added to you village which create puzzle as you try to fit them together so they can work in synergy with each other.  The rounds are broken into 4 seasons the first three are similar however, winter tiles are determined by tiles received by players at the beginning thus determining a game strategy.   This game is so satisfying as you build a village from nothing.  The mix of game mechanics is so unique and works so well. one would think the game would be complicated with so many mechanics but the rules are easy and intuitive conversely, game play is deep and vast. It even scales well down to two players which is unusual for a game with bidding.  Two or three player games feel very similar and are equally enjoyable.  The best of a very good series or Key-games.

RATING: 8
Restored my faith in the modern heavy euro. Unlike baroque conversion engines like Terra Mystica, the interlocking systems of Keyflower fit together tightly and put pressure on every choice you make. 

Keyflower isn't about making a sequence of opaque value conversions. The choices in keyflower are stark and remorseless. Bid on a building to add to your village, or use the building power. Use the power, and you lose your meeple to the owner. Bids have to follow suit (colour). Concentrate on two meeple colors and lose the flexibility of bidding in all three colors. Stockpile goods or use them to upgrade buildings. Don't neglect your transport capacity. And unlike say, Le Havre, the decision to use an opponent's building is a tough one. 

Keyflower features some really nice touches around the periphery. The rare green meeple. The choice of special winter tiles. The turn order dilemma - first choice of boats and resources, or first placement next turn.

Nothing seems extraneous. And you're not playing away undisturbed on your own tableau. It takes sharp elbows, bold bids, and ruthless timing to come out ahead. Tremendous fun.

RATING: 8.1
I enjoyed this at my first game. Keyflower is quite simple, it is played over four rounds with each round consisting of an interesting worker placement in which workers can work on tiles in the market or in your village, or other villages, or bid for tiles in the market, or for ship loads of workers coming in. The auction mechanic and the placement are relaxed (you get to re-place your outbid workers and can take a space a second time if you pay more workers).
All sorts of different things score points and you need to work out which ones are best to concentrate on. There is plenty to think about here and a lot of elements that I have seen in other worker placement games but executed with a bit more elegance. There is nothing original here but its nice to see a worker placement game that tries to add elegance rather than complexity. I would certainly play again and expect there is enough depth to be worth a lengthy exploration.

RATING: 8
I've not played any of the other "key" games, and have little interest in them to be honest - but I heard good things about this one, and am glad I asked for a copy. It is a wonderful game - both bidding and worker placement are standard, hackneyed mechanics (and I don't particularly like auction games, as a general rule), but the way this manages to combine the two into one integrated mechanism is really something special. By the last round of the game, everyone is typically doing long chains of actions to get the things they want, while also trying to ensure they win the point-scoring tiles which they need. While there isn't any one mechanic here which is new, overall this feels very fresh and different from every other Euro I've played. And the randomness of the tile draw ensures great replayability. In fact, I can't think of anything bad to say about this game - I did wonder if downtime might be a problem at the larger player counts, but I've played once with 5 and once with 6 and didn't notice a problem, even though most of the others were new to the game.

I don't play this game very often, and it's fallen off my list of absolute favourites - but I'm still always delighted to play it.

RATING: 6
Será que estou ficando chato? Estava tão empolgado com o Keyflower, tinha achado que essa inovação na mecânica de você usar trabalhadores tanto para dar lance em tiles quanto para executar as ações deles (inclusive enquanto estão sendo leiloados!) era tão, mas tão sensacional, que nada podia fazer o jogo ser ruim.

A mecânica é bacana mesmo, mas ela passa razoavelmente despercebida durante o jogo. O foco da sua atenção está mais nas ações dos tiles do que em qualquer coisa. E as ações são conversões abstratas: cubinho cinza vira amarelo, pecinha marrom vira 3 cubinhos pretos, meeple azul vira 2 meeples verdes, tudo tão seco que no final do jogo você não sabe nem o nome da infinidade de tiles (serralheria, igreja, estábulo? não importa) e nem o que cada cubinho representa.

Ou seja, a mecânica inovadora fica meio escanteada, o importante mesmo é otimização e conversão. Desconfio que vai fazer sucesso com quem gosta de Le Havre e Ora et Labora, que não é meu caso.

Sobre esses dois jogos, acho que o Keyflower tem a vantagem de ser mais curto. Nossa Learning Session com 5 pessoas ficou abaixo das 3h, com muita consulta de manual, o que me parece razoável.

Ele talvez tenha uma desvantagem de dar mais poder pra quem está na frente. Mas difícil dizer isso depois de uma partida só, pode ser que simplesmente o vencedor tenha jogado muito bem.

Jogaria outras pra ver se a impressão é desfeita e pra quem não veio hoje ter a oportunidade. Mas a primeira impressão é de passar adiante.

RATING: 6
Lots of tiles in the box, but only a subset are used in any one game.  This should allow for diverse games as the situations aren't always the same.  Meeples used multiple ways - currency for bidding on tiles or worker placement to trigger tile effects.  This adds a bit of meeple management to the game as workers spent on tiles don't come home at the end of the season.  While the boats do bring more, one's mix of meeples can be in somewhat of a flux and must be managed.  Lots of options for each player so one isn't necessarily cut out of the game.  The heart of the game is an auction system that keeps players engaged with the benefit that the powers of the tile can be used while the tile is still being bid for.  (No buy first, use later here.)

RATING: 9
I don´t usually enjoy "Eurogames", but without doubt, this is one of the best and original "Eurogames" I´ve in my collection, not in terms of appearance, of course, but in terms of mechanics. The Graphic Design of this game is sober, just fair, to say something positive... Materials are good quality, except the player screens, which are nice but flimsy. Anyway, above these little details, where this game really shines is how well mixture of its mechanics works: it´s brilliant in this point! Everything works smoothly and gracefully. Options for strategies are very wide and open. And it's an addictive game! Everyone says that it plays well at 2 players, at 4 or at 6 players. I can imagine this is true. We, for now, have only played it with 2 players, and we love it.

RATING: 7
Quite a unique game: a medium to medium-heavy euro with no negative interaction, that plays best with six and still lasts under two hours. A unique bidding mechanic, quite well integrated tile-laying and resource production & consumption makes for a quite interesting game which nevertheless leaves an impression of deficiency - the game is just too short to achieve much. During 4 game rounds, you'll buy and place a couple of tiles, produce some resources and move them around your village and that's it. What's more, it doesn't scale down that well, as with fewer players not all tiles will enter play, and the ability to get the tile scoring for resources you produce makes a big difference in points. If you're not sure if a tile that suits your strategy will be drawn in the future, you can't plan much and the game is too short to alter your strategy or fix mistakes.

RATING: 9
Just keeps getting better and better with more play. Started off as an eight, then nudged up to a nine and now it's just a pleasure to bring out and get my teeth stuck into. This and Le Havre really nail it and hence a ten in my book. (Agricola would be up there too if it wasn't for the dull scoring objectives.) Nothing does it better than these two in this category.

Easily my favourite Key game. Loads of interesting choices to make all within the framework of a simple ruleset and a thought provoking bidding and building usage mechanic. I really enjoy the cat and mouse game played as players wait untilt he right moment to pounce on a bid or building. Sometimes it's straight away to use your colour advantage (maybe the building is just too tempting), while sometimes you delay to the last possible action your upgrade and subsequent activation of a tile.

While there's plenty to think about it's not energy wasted on too many rules. That said, a cheat sheet for players with the tile descriptions would be handy for players to grab hold of. Rules are clear, but a bit of an effort to read through the first time.

The artwork is superb. It's art I like that I get to play with.Nothing could be better than that.

Altogether a wonderful package. The time doesn't drag, but it doesn't feel like my brain is melting either. Points seem to be close. 12 tiles provided for each season always means plenty remain unused from game to game. This along with meeple and tile draw should ensure that each game will be quite different from the last. Terrific stuff.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
[u][b]Why is it awesome?[/b][/u]

Keyflower is my absolute favorite game of all time.  Like many of the best euros out there, the rules are not overbearing but the strategies are deep and varied.  Because the tiles that come out in every season are varied, you can be assured that no two games will ever play out the same way.  

Its gorgeously illustrated with colorful water-color-ish drawings, and the graphic design elements and symbols are very clear and easy to understand. But don't let the sunny disposition fool you - this is a MEAN game.  You can lock someone out of their own tile by putting the right amount (or color) of meeples on their tile to take it's action just because [i]you know[/i] they needed to use that tile to do something cool.  Or you can screw someone out of that scoring tile they put out in winter just to deny them some points.  Other people might see this as a negative, but those people are frail, wispy little creatures that can't handle a little in-your-face interaction.  

Bottom line is I'm sitting there every single game grinning like a maniac because the game is simply brilliant.  There is plenty of player interaction, but no player elimination.  I always feel like I'm in the game, even when I'm losing horribly - maybe not in the running for winning, but always able to do something interesting.  Every game is like a little puzzle I'm trying to solve based on the available tiles.

I could go on all day about this game.  Love it.  LOVE IT.  Possibly too much...

RATING: 9

You can use other players' tiles, but then they get to keep the worker for the next round/season. You can work to get end-game bonuses, or just work to upgrade your own village. So many paths to choose.

It's really wonderful. Easily a 9.

RATING: 8
Keyflower is an amazing game that combines worker placement and bidding seamlessly. The mechanics are brilliant, the tile distribution offers a great deal of variety, and the game scales well between player counts all the way from 2 to 6. The only issue with the scaling is that higher player count games can become unbearable if multiple players suffer from significant AP. The main problem with the base game is that it has a too much emphasis on the winter tiles.

The Farmers expansion adds variety, but overall isn't a very good expansion. It's functional, but doesn't make the game better.

The Merchants is a great expansion that reduces the emphasis on the winter tiles by giving you other ways to generate a lot of points.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very nice worker placement/bidding game with modular tiles to build your village with. 

You get meeples of various colors, bid on turn order and tiles to build your village, then collect the tiles and various boats with new meeples based on your turn order. You then build your village, use resources from the tiles to gain VP by upgrading your tiles, and after 4 turns, the most VP wins.

Very simple to learn, and extremely fun with 4 people.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Played twice with 3P and once with 4P. I am a sucker for multi-layered, Homesteaders-style auctions and the ability to use your opponent's buildings to gain benefits, so Keyflower is a no-brainer for me. Players build up engines by bidding on building tiles, and each season offers something different for the engine. If you don't have the necessary resources, you can always activate your opponents' buildings at a cost to get what you need. 

The auction for the tiles are unique as there are 4 different types of currency available. Once a bid for a tile is started, all following bids must use the same type of currency. Incidentally, the bidding currency also happen to be the workers whom players use to activate buildings, so players have to strike a balance in how they spend their workers.

One of the best things about Keyflower is that it delivers a lot of depth for the amount of time to play it (60-90 mins for a 3-4 player game). It does not, however, feature the simplest of rules to get into, so despite its intuitive gameplay it still takes at least one game to get used to it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

+ Keyflower: The Merchants

A brutal, cutthroat euro. This is a game where you can never rest easy, and you have to look closely at what your opponents are doing. 50% of the game is building your engine, the other 50% is shutting down your opponents. And the game gives you all the tools you need to mess with your opponent - from the unique bidding mechanism to the way tiles come out in the game. I think 2p is the most confrontational, with 4p being more sedate.

The Merchants expansion really adds a lot to the base game. It gives a few more ways to score points during the game so you don't feel too bad as you get repeatedly stabbed by your opponents :)

[20+ plays]

RATING: 6
[2013-02-09]
Nos, ha valaki sokallja a Le Havre, Ora et Labora lehetőségeit, akkor az ne kezdjen a Keyflowerrel, ugyanis ebben a játékban hihetetlen mennyiségű lehetőség van, ami szerintem már átláthatatlan. Én úgy gondolom optimálisan lehetetlen játszani benne, maximum egy általános stratégiára lehet törekedni. A játékosok négy évszakon át licitálnak Catan szerű lapkákra, amiből a városukat építik. Minden lapkának van egy tulajdonsága, sőt fejlesztés után még ezen tulajdonság jobb formája is rendelkezésre áll. Ezen felül minden lapka a használható is, akárkinél és akárhol is van. Különböző színű embereinkkel licitálunk vagy használunk lapokat. A 4 körös játék már második köre is szinte átláthatatlan lehetőségmennyiséggel operál. Nagyon sok ilyen lap kerül elő és nehezen optimalizálható figuráink jó helyre rakása. Persze minden ér valamit, de szerintem annak ellenére, hogy az első játékunkban nagyon szoros végeredmény született simán elképzelhető nagy vereség is. Szaharaszáraz a játék, semmilyen téma nem jön át, ez full optimalizálás. Megmondom őszintén csak azért nem lep meg e tavalyi esseni játék sikere, mert tudom a fő geekek ilyen játékokra vágynak. Nekem, aki már tényleg elég komoly játékokon is túl van már sok volt. Nem katasztrófa játék a Keyflower, a licitmechanizmus és embergazdálkodás kifejezetten tetszik a játékban, de a sok lehetőség megöli az élményt. Nálam ez gyenge 6-os csak.

RATING: 8
Nice auction game.  It clips along at a great pace, so much so that you frequently are wishing that the game were a little longer as you are never going to do everything you need to before it comes to completion.  Simple easy design, but there seems to be some real depth here.  The varying tiles will keep returning players on their toes.  Still, this is the kind of game that rewards player knowing all potential tiles available. 

Something that is a little niggly, is that it requires that players pay attention and remember who has which buildings, especially when tiles start getting covered by wood.  You can easily miss good opportunities, by not expending the energy to track everything.

The only reason why I can't in good conscience rate this higher is that you can't be sure that you can ever upgrade or even use some of the tiles that you work to get.  

All in all, lots of fun.  I think this is his best game since Reef Encounter.

RATING: 5
Clever but cumbersome mash up of auctions and worker placement. I'll grant that Keyflower is unique. And it's interactive enough, especially for a worker placement game. But there's an awfully high barrier to entry with too much tedious cube pushing for my taste. Keyflower reminds me of Troyes, but with a level of complexity closer to Terra Mystica. As in Troyes, half of the difficulty is deciphering the euroglyphics on the tiles. Even if you think you know what a tile does, refer to the rulebook anyway because it's full of exceptions and inconsistencies. That's fine if there's a thematic rationale. Here there's none. As in Terra Mystica, there are a few too many subsystems that seem to have been added in because they could be, not because they were necessary.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
1 play, 4er

Key Flower is an interesting worker-placement type game, sort of: it uses workers in a pretty unique way -- they come in different colors, which limit where they may be used, and there's a nice economy of getting new workers and using them most efficiently. Bidding is simple and works well. And every game should be different: you use a random subset each season. I'm not sure I liked it as much as Key Market, nor as much as Myrmes (which has a worker mechanic that feels a bit similar) ... but this is certainly worth additional plays!

--- Setup ---
There's a bag full of meeples (red, yellow, or blue), as well as a pile of green meeples on the table; players each start with a random mix of 8 (for 4er game, at least), hidden behind their screens. Piles of resources (wood, iron, stone, gold) are placed in Supply, as well as a pile of face down Skill tiles. A number (n) of boats are placed nearby and stocked with Skills and meeples (from bag) as pictured on the tile. Players get a random set of 3 winter tiles; worth VP at end-game scoring if you satisfy varying conditions. Each player also has a home tile, with the ability to upgrade 1 tile and move 1 resource 1 space. There are 4 seasons in the game, each following the same pattern (except winter). 

Each Season
At the start of each round (season), place a number of tiles (according to player count) on display, matching the current season (Spring, Summer, Fall). For Winter, players choose 1-3 of their tiles from Setup and place those on Display instead. There are also turn order tiles that are always on display.

Your Turn
On your turn, you may pass, OR place worker(s) on any one tile, to use the tile's power, or on the edge of a tile on Display to bid on it. Once any color meeple has been placed at/on a tile, all other meeples must be the same color. If activating a tile, must place at least 1 more meeple there than are already present and may never exceed 6 meeple/tile limit. Abilities include things like earning skills tiles or resources (if activating another player's tile, place resources on your home tile; if your own tile, resources go on that tile; skills always go behind your screen). gaining new meeples or converting them to green, moving resources, upgrading tiles, earning end-game VP, etc. If one of your previous bids was raised, you may move all those meeples as a group to another location (adding meeples from behind your screen if needed). When upgrading a tile, must have the necessary resources on that tile; flip it over to the other side (only upgraded side is worth VP; also, better powers on that side). If everyone passes consecutively, the round ends.

End of Round
First, all overbid meeples are returned to their owners (according to the edge where you placed your bid). All meeples on tiles in your Display also go behind your screen. All tiles won from Display are taken, along with any meeples ON them (from people using tile power): meeples go behind your screen, and tiles added to your personal display such that all edges match (fields, water, or roads). Then, resolve turn order tiles (there are n-1 of them): in order, choose one of the boat tiles and take all meeples/skill tiles on it. The player winning #1 goes first next round. Some boats will be flipped over (according to icon on tile; should match next season).

At end of Winter, players add the turn order tiles to their displays, as well as the boat tiles -- they give additional VP.

--- End of Game ---
After winter, players get one final move step where they may move resources (except to some tiles from Fall which give VP for having resources on them). Then, simply tally your VP from all your tiles; most VP wins!

RATING: 3.5
I don't often rate after one play, but I think this one warrants it:

This game is OVERWHELMING to learn, but isn't very complicated. That's a red flag for me... I don't much like games that utterly bury you with *stuff* right from the get go, with a huge spiderweb of options and strategies, to the point that there's quite literally no discernible course of action.

Sure, there's a game here, sure, it's probably fantastic, but it requires such extensive input from the players to get there I just don't see it as a casual or even semi-casual game. Everyone needs to know it -- and know it WELL -- for the play experience to be satisfying.

Not worth the time investment at this point of my life, but I enjoyed it well enough... would I play again? Meh, maybe. Probably not with my current game group, as I don't think it will grip enough people who will want to play it enough that I would be satisfied with the group's understanding of the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
One of only two Breese "Key" games I've played.  This is a very good and very deep game.  There are a lot of moving parts, which I love, and some pretty original elements.

The game is a pseudo-worker placement game with a pretty prevalent bidding mechanic and home area city building.  Like I said, there are a lot of moving parts.

Each game round is a season and each season tiles are placed out for players to use and bid for.  The first of the unique mechanics in this game is each player bids with meeples and the meeples are in 4 different colors.  Bidding on a tile has to be done in the same color, so if I bid 2 blue meeples, you have to bid 3 blue meeples in order to outbid me.  Also, only 3 of the colors are easily available.  The fourth color, green, is in shorter supply and only granted when using certain tiles and therefore gives a player more bidding power.

The use of these tiles is also pretty original.  Instead of bidding for a tile or after the tile is placed in a player's home area you can place meeples directly on it to use its effect.  Using a tile also has to be done with the same color meeples as used to bid on the tile or use it previously.  And the owner of that tile (or winner of the bid) gets to keep any meeples on it for use in later rounds.

I was thoroughly impressed by how deep and original this game was and at the convention in which I played this I was ready to jump up and buy a copy halfway through.  The end game scoring scared me off though.  There was already almost too much complexity for me to think I could introduce it to my more casual game group but I enjoyed it so much I was hoping I could make it work.  But the end game scoring brought a lot more complexity to the table that just pushed it over the edge.  I will still play this game whenever given the chance but it just wouldn't ever get played on my shelf.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
因為米寶多、圖版漂亮而喜歡上這款遊戲。機制確實很棒，很耐玩，支援的人數也很有彈性，同時（我相信）也不會有人數差異大而遊戲體驗差很多的情況（目前僅開過兩人場）。然而設置太久，桌面需求太大，上手門檻又偏高一些，卻是難以忽略的缺點。

第一次自己試跑的時候就有發現，雖然是競標版塊是遊戲很重要的一部分，但總共只會獲得4次，而且每季結束會一次拿到不只一片版塊，對於新手玩家而言不但不直覺，還會增加拼放的難度。同時拿到的版塊有很大的機會會在下回合才開始大量放置工人在上面，玩家一開始也會不習慣把工人擺去別人家（第一次遊戲經驗證實有沒有這個選項差別很大），算挺不直覺的。反過來說，如果可以逐漸掌握這些抉擇，《大五月花號》可以玩的選擇就多很多，策略也可以深一些。

但真正讓我評到6分的原因，恐怕是設置超級久，桌子也要超級大......建議想入手的朋友多買一個抽取袋給工具指示物用，以及為資源準備個透明盒。

應該會再找策略咖多試幾場，再來補心得吧！
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
I'm a fan of Mr Breese's "Key" series. To be honest I like Keythedral very much and Key Market so and so. The others (Keydom I don't know at all) are not welcome in my gaming table. But I still love the concept of a non-confrontational but hugely interactive resource planning kind of game.
Keyflower is one of the kind. Players bid for tiles in order to get the best. These tiles should be added to each player's city. Apart from bidding for a tile it is also possible for players to use the tiles, activating them.
The main secret for the huge success of Keyflower is the complexity of each decision allied with the rule's simplicity. Everything is so simple to understand. The main focus is on the decision itself, like every game should be.
I have only played the game once so far but it seems to be the best of the series until now. And of course, one of the best Essen 12 releases. Highly recommended.

(G/U)

RATING: 7.5
Interesting little mishmash auction game. Tiles to place in your village are acquired by auction, the novelties being that subsequent bids must be the same colour and that losing bids can be moved, a la Amun-Re, to another tile. But the real trick is that tiles can be used even while they are being auctioned, with all subsequent uses of a tile taking more workers than the one before it -- and the player who wins the tile takes the workers on it too.

The result is a sort of combination worker placement auction, with tradeoffs around tile ownership value (positional implications of tiles, future worker earnings potential etc.), and action value/timing. 

It works very well, with hidden goals -- that, once public, are auctioned off! -- to guide overall strategy and many options for tactical variation. The different colours/currencies are a great touch, especially the rarer green workers.

The level of randomness in the tile and worker draws is perhaps a little too much for me, but there's definitely a lot to like. It reminds me strongly of Ora et Labora, but with a far more interactive method of building acquisition and activation. 

As a bonus, it's utterly gorgeous: possibly Juliet Breese's best work. The unique player screens are especially nice.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Fun Euro game that has some interesting choices in how bidding works with 3 different currencies. The theme is a bit pasted on, but it still works in a sense.

My only complains are that the game is too short and the green meeple are extremely overpowered. 

I wish it was 4 rounds and then a final scoring round instead of 3 and then scoring. Doesn't feel long enough to build up a good engine.

Also, the rule about green meeples being used for bidding going into the meeple draw bag is extremely weird. It can lead to random swings of luck if someone manages to pull one considering how powerful they are. I wonder if the game would have been better without the green meeples at all... or some way to make them easier to get or just have them act as a wild color.

Overall, it is a fun game with some interesting strategic and tactical choices. Not really a fan of the connecting locals and scoring points based off of that (another needless mechanism). Should play fairly differently after each game though considering the number of tiles available. Finally, there is a bit of randomness and luck in what tile combinations come out. It makes the game interesting, but can wildly favor one player over another.

The tiles that you won comes in your village. When you use your own tile the worker comes back to you. When you use someones tile the worker comes to that player. When you use a neutral tile the worker comes to the player who win the bid for that tile.

RATING: N/A
Greg
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I enjoy Keyflower tremendously, but don't get to play it often enough. At it's most basic, it's about collecting resources for victory points. This alone is nothing spectacular. However, to collect resources, players are bidding for tiles to add to their individual villages. Players bid by using Meeples in one of three main colors. Once a bid has been placed on a particular tile, other players may only increase the bid by using Meeples of the same color. On top of this, there are multiple tiles up for bid at the same time. Thus, there are multiple bidding wars going on simultaneously. In some ways, the bidding mechanic reminds me of [thing=96913]Lancaster[/thing] in that, once you are outbid, you can either increase your bid or move your losing Meeples to another location. There is a lot of back and forth, vying for valuable tiles, which can be very engaging.

I also appreciate how everyone can make use of all the tiles, even tiles in other players' villages. Thus, there is quite a bit of interaction in what could have otherwise been just another multi-player solitaire game. If anything, though, the plethora of options can be overwhelming to keep track of. While it wasn't an issue in our game, I wonder if an AP-prone player wouldn't bog this game down tremendously. As is, with a full six players, the game is already quite long -- probably too long if you're playing with new players.

With four player who know the rules, there is much less downtime, or even room for downtime. It's a much shorter game. Much more enjoyable! The auction continues to be cutthroat and exciting. Two is even quicker, but you lose some of the tension that comes from more complex auctions and greater interaction.

Overall, it's a wonderful game -- and I haven't even mentioned the theme and artwork, all of which I find absolutely charming. The attention to detail, down to individual houses that serve as player screens are lovely. While I wish those same player screen stayed together better, that's about the only complaint I can even think to make about the game's components. Top notch.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7

Also a small quibble I have to note ... the trend of games with action spots you can use in OTHER PLAYERS' AREAS is not a great one. It's hard to see, fiddly and slows the game. OK - rant over. I'll certainly play Keyflower again, but for me it's not a keeper.

RATING: 8
Now that I have multiple plays, this game absolutely deserves a 10 from me.

I love how the game forces you to adjust your strategy as new tiles appear/don't appear. Then it throws in the decisions of when to bid on tiles you want, when to color-lock tiles your opponents want, when to start using tiles in your area / vs your opponents. There are so many decisions, it can seem overwhelming, but because the core game is so simple, it works together perfectly.

Add in a lack of a single dominant strategy, and a beautiful auction/worker placement mechanic, and you get a perfect score for me.

RATING: 8
[thing=122515][/thing] is a medium-heavy game (for me) in which players are bidding for tiles, activating tiles (even the ones players are bidding on), all in order to make resources (wood, coal, gold, meeples, and so on), develop buildings and acquiring victory points.

The game allows for many strategies, and the use of meeples (and their colors) to activate tiles (even the ones the player are bidding on) and also bid on them, is something I never seen before, and is necessary to think really well in what to do, in which order, because, for instance, you can activate a tile, then use the resources gained by this to win the bid of this same tile, taking the tile and the meeples you used to activate it! It is just great.

The point that makes this game stand out is: once a color of meeple is used (for bidding or activation), everyone else, even the initial player, [b]must[/b] use the same color. This simple thing is awesome. This adds a whole level of attention, interaction, blocking, gathering and strategy to [thing=122515][/thing]. I loved it! Every time during play, we were all grumbling, cursing and hoping that the others would chose a good color for you, and it was a joy, when someone activated a building and you go there, with an handful of meeples to basically take it for you. "How many red meeples you could possibly have, right?"... "Oh, dear."

There is an important spatial element in [thing=122515][/thing]: the place in which you put the tiles matter, since you will have to take the goods from the tile that produced them to others, in order to upgrade the building (and, with this, making it better to do things and also most will be worth VPs). Also, in the beginning of the game you receive 2 tiles that, in the last season (winter), you will have to put (at least one) for auction, and these tiles usually be some strong VP earners, if you prepare for them - this gives some north to your overall strategy, but you are free to just ignore them and try other things, vying for other tiles (but, then, you won't be able to know if they will actually be put for auction in the winter).

In five players - with some AP (I'm to be blamed for part of this) - the game took a little over two hours. It doesn't hurt that [thing=122515][/thing] is a beautiful game - it is sorrowful that the icons take so much space of that pretty tiles.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
The meeple bid and action selection mechanic is appealing, as is the availability of everyone's tableau. But the economy is hidden and there's not a clear feedback loop. Tension fails from wasteful thinking, design red herrings, and lack of focus. The road system, for example, felt vestigal as one really couldn't choose tiles for that content and the specifics of that network felt unnecessary. Irritating and phoney. Rather than adding a pleasing sub-system I felt it as drift.

The resources are functionally indistinguishable, so it's sort of a kinetic set collection game, and routing these things to their right tile to assemble in VP earning clusters feels clumsy. I do like the idea of the lightness of a commonly built logistic network creating value for the fine auction, but it misses from lack of commitment and abstraction.

A tactical drift through jetsom and flotsam that is too much a burden to pull together. Another game that separates the players with VP splatter, however disguised.

I wonder if this would have been designed as building a common village, with resources & roads interlocked and clear, if the interesting auction idea could have a better environment and the thematic latching become coherent.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A very well-designed game that deserves all the accolades and hype heaped upon by gamers after its debut. This is a gamer's game with many subtle nuances to game play and multiple routes to victory. Like many other heavier strategy games, Keyflower requires players to have a few initial games under their belts to appreciate all the potential synergy and combinations available from the tiles, especially the winter scoring tiles. Things I liked about the game include the novel use of meeples for both bidding and activation. I particularly like how usage of meeples becomes more restricted as you play them on the tiles. The dilemma of playing all the meeples at once to win the bid or and to deter competition, or to slowly increase each bid is particularly agonizing. I also loved how the winter scoring tiles are introduced by individual players selecting specific tiles up for bid in the last round. Even if you desperately want a particular scoring tile in the final round and put it up for bid, it doesn't mean you will win the bid. It is absolutely heart-breaking to lose a tile which you have been working on scoring for the full game during the bidding phase. Some will see this as a weakness of the game but the final round bidding has to be carefully planned and executed to perfection in order for things to go your way. Several aspects of the game I dislike: the rulebook could be better organized and written. Yes, it is not the worst rulebook, but the organization is confusing and the choice of words can be murky. There are dozens of rules threads in the forum seeking clarification of the bidding process. It really needs to be cleaned up. We were also quite lost with the purpose of the green meeples since it was never brought up or discussed in any part of the rulebook. At the very least, a passing mention on how the green meeples could also be used for bidding and activation would be helpful. Finally, I am also not a huge fan of the dozens or so scenario to resolve turn-order bidding if some of the turn order tiles remain uncontested. It is confusing and fiddly at the very least. Nit picky details for a well-designed game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

Still need to play the Merchants expansion and definitely need to play this one more.

RATING: 6.5
Has several aspects I dislike but manages to be pretty enjoyable in spite of this.

The rules are generally inelegant, with lots of niggling little exceptions that are easy to forget or confuse.  I'd much rather see a game be tweaked to remove excessive complexity rather than just making up more rules to fix balance issues.

Road placement will easily wreck you semi-randomly on your first few plays because you won't know what to expect in later rounds - for example, simply having 2 adjacent roads going towards an empty space will make the vast majority of fall and winter tiles unplayable there.

The single-color bidding mechanic and 1-2-3 workers required for an action feels very nice and is probably the best aspect of the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Engine Building games typically fall flat with me, but there are a lot of interesting decisions in this game.  The fact that you constantly have to decide how to balance your actions between bidding, upgrading, shuffling resources, and simply trying to bog down your opponent.  They all use the same resource,  so you really have to weigh every option. 

I think what makes this game especially great for me is that there is nonstop player interaction which is atypical for a Euro.  You are constantly playing off opponents, blocking the, exporting their tiles, faking out bids, trying to make them pay more while setting yourself up for big payouts.

Tons of choices and constant decisions to be made.  Not too AP inducing aside from the final winter round.  I love that you're playing your opponents, and not just the game.

Keyflower has steadily been climbing up my ratings with each play.  It's a fantastic mix of strategy, interaction, easy of setup and quick play.

For somebody out there looking for a light-to-medium Euro, this is a very good choice.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Keyflower is a bidding/tile laying game. On your turn, you place meeples either on a tile to use it, or beside a tile as a bid in an attempt to win it, and place it in your personal village area at the end of a round. The trick is, there are four different colours of meeples, functionally three different kinds of currency, as other players must "follow suit" in order to bid on or use the same tile you did. A starting bid of 3 yellow means you must beat it with 4+ yellow, and so forth.

Meeple management is key (heh). A meeple placed on a tile to use it gets kept by the owner of that tile. So, if you place it on your own tile to get a benefit, you get to keep the meeples for next round to bid or use again. If you place it on your opponent's tiles, you get the short term benefit, but they now have extra meeples to outbid you next round. You can even place them on a tile that's currently up for bidding, but the winner of the tile gets to keep those meeples as well. Meeples used to win a tile are lost, which can really leave you short next round.

The tile laying aspects of the game are quite fun as well. You must match features when building, roads to roads, blank to blank and so forth. This is important, as tiles generate their resources on their own tile. So you often need to transport resources from one tile to another along your road system. Early tiles have lots of roads, but this diminishes as the game goes along, so getting goods to late tiles can be tough. Resources can be used to upgrade tiles, which flips them over for points or better abilities, such as a mill produces 3 wood instead of 1 wood.

There's a ton of "think" in this game, lots of agonizing decisions about where to put your guys and what moves to do, and it does so in a very short time. Games just fly by, as there's only 4 rounds. Highly recommended.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
One play so far. I can understand why this is in the top-25 at the moment, but I understand well why this is not my favorite. Players build a scoring engine and who gets most of the points, wins the game. To be good at this, you need to know (at least approximately) what kind of tiles there are coming later in the game - so if you play once every now and then, you won't be good at this.

The components are good, I mean they are clear and they show the information nicely, but they aren't actually pretty.

If I would be playing more than at the moment, I'd probably played this more and then this would feel better, but as I have limited time, I won't. Most likely there's always something more interesting than this quite abstract engine building game to play, so I most likely will skip this. Not a bad game, but not just my game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
UPDATE: Tried the game again with the view that the short playtime is a feature, not a bug. We were less vicious about the bidding and this improved the game a lot. With two newbie players, it provides an Agricola-like experience in under an hour. Nice quirky replayable game with beautiful graphics. Impossible to dislike, even by someone who normally hates resource optimisation games. Rating increased to eight.

After two plays, it's 'meh'. It finishes too quickly before you can get an engine going or do much at all, the geographical portion is basically worthless, etc. It feels like half a game... No desire to play again. If I want resource optimisation, I own Agricola. If I want auctions with two, I own Metropolys.

After one play with two, I've no idea what to rate this game. I've graded it 7.5 since it's a good game and I enjoyed playing - happy I own it and will suggest it again. The grading may rise or fall depending on how much depth it has with repeat plays.

It's dry and, if you want a game dripping with theme, it's not that game. I didn't think *wow, this is the best game ever*. 

It's an interesting game with moderately simple rules, an overly complex rulebook, and a bunch of tension caused by the auction mechanic. There are lots of options, e.g. focusing on upgrading your village and hope to scupper your opponent's winter tiles, focus on scoring massive on your winter tiles, etc.

It's competent and it *looks* to be replayable, but since the resources are broadly interchangeable, it's not clear how much long-term variety there is. The Keyflower: Farmers expansion looks interesting.

It doesn't feel like any game I own except, possibly, Glen More.

RATING: 8.5
2 - [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/tag/6p/user/Vroenis]6 players[/url].
Teaching: 60 - 120 minutes.
Mastery: 45 - 90 minutes.
Standard box.
[url=http://boardgamegeek.com/geektag.php?tags=embassy_%26_outpost&username=Vroenis&action=usertags]Embassy_&_Outpost[/url]

[url=http://boardgamegeek.com/tag/worker_placement/user/Vroenis]Worker placement[/url].
Bidding.
Player city building.
[url=http://boardgamegeek.com/tag/resource_management/user/Vroenis]Resource management[/url].
Medium-heavy weight.
Medium player interaction.
Low-medium theme application.

Keyflower is a heavier game but it plays six which is nice, one more than Agricola. It can get pretty cutthroat in the bidding phases and players will need to have plans and backup plans for the coming turns. First games might be a little tough but with perseverance there's a lot of fun to be had in the crunch. As it's slightly more mechanical than games like Agricola that may seem more logical, those who favour strategy will enjoy it the most.

One of the strongest things in Keyflower's favour is its high level of crunch offered in a significantly shorter play time in comparison to other strategy games.
