RATING: 5
My first 5-players game of Keyflower, unfortunately, has been an unbearably slow and boring experience of mechanical transformation of wooden pieces and moving of tiles, deprived of any faint appearance of fun.
I can't understand how such a game can reach the present ranking to the expence and detriment of many other wonderful games.
The theme is pasted on, non-existent and unhelpful; your strategy can be easily messed up nonsensically by other players just by using a meeple of the wrong colour. The length of the game is more than 2 hours in 5 players and it is really really too much, considering that the first of 4 turns ends quite quickly. The fact that each player needs to pass to end a turn makes room for unbearably long final turns.
The one and only good thing about Keyflower is the originality of the auction mechanic that at the beginning made me think this could be quite a good game. Alas, not so. It just ruined my weekly gaming night.

RATING: 7
I was pleasantly surprised by Keyflower.  I like the way the bidding works and how you go about acquiring your workers/currency each round.  I like the spatial aspect of how your village builds up.  And I like that it's short enough (an hour or so) that I didn't get tired of it.  It's got a lot of nice interactions, but if it was twice as long, I think it would get tedious.  The physical design of the game is also excellent.  Unlike, say, Ora et Labora, I can look over the whole table at the couple dozen unique power tiles in play on the table and know what every one of them does without picking them up and in my first play of the game--no tiny text on tiny cards.

Very tight gameplay with (generally...) quick turns. Love building up my little engine (city) only to have someone block me out of it with greens in the last season.

A gem of a game, with very unique mechanics. And while I'll definitely say it's not for everyone, with the right group you'll just want to play it over and over.

Willing to give this one another go. The first learning play at an FLGS were fraught with issues - loud restaurant, etc.

* After first play w/wife - she really enjoyed it and it proved even more enjoyable for me.  Yeah this game is indeed excellent.

The interactions w/6p: one would think that it would slog and drag but with people running their villages as well as bidding, the seasonal portion didn't really take that long.  2h game w/4newbs and 2 vets.  Granted this could change with fully experienced players dynamically passing/jumping back in next turn/etc.

RATING: 10
GAME
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.8
This is my favorite game and it primarily revolves around an excellently designed auction/bidding mechanic with tile laying and resource management that only help to sharpen and enhance the overall design!  The game lasts four seasons, each with their own unique sets of tiles that come out in new variations from game to game.  This adds a tremendous amount of variability and the feel of every game playing very different outside of the core mechanics. 

The major aspect of the game, the auction/bidding mechanic, is amazingly done and adds a tension that is unmatched by any other game.  You are trying to obtain hex tiles to add to your village.  These tiles allow you to continue routes in your village and open up worker placement spots that are specific to your village only.  Every season you will want to purchase tiles that you want because they will give you valuable action spots that you can benefit from, and also entice other players to send their workers to your village.  The catch to obtaining these tiles in the auction is that once a tile has been bid on with a colored meeple (blue, red, yellow, and the rarest being green) that sets the color to be bid on for that tile.  So, once a player has placed a bid using blue meeples, the only other choice to outbid that opponent is by using a greater number of blue meeples.  This means that the game has four unique currencies being used and manipulated by players during the auction and when placing workers.  During the auction, you can also play on a tile within the auction by putting your meeple onto a tile that hasn't been played on or by playing the same "set" color onto an already used or bid for tile.  

Another brilliant aspect to the design is that while the auction is taking place, you can use your turn not only to bid for a tile to add to your village at the end of the round or play on one of the tiles in the auction, but you can also play onto tiles in your village and your opponents village that have been obtained in previous rounds.  Sometimes getting a new tile to add to your village comes secondary to being able to use the tile action spots in your or your opponent's village.  Many of these provide valuable resources  and abilities such as skill tiles, meeples, goods, or the chance to transport goods to locations and possibly upgrade the tiles within your village.  You may want to transport goods onto other tiles so that you can upgrade the tile for a more powerful action spot and/or to get endgame points. However, when you transport your goods you must follow the layout of the roads on the tiles and can only move them as far as the action points allow you to send them.  

Another strong aspect of the game is the balance between strategy and tactics.  At the beginning of the game you are given a number of winter tiles that give you a set of strategic end game goals to pursue.  Sometimes you will focus on one and sometimes you will focus on several.  When winter comes, you will be forced to play at least one tile from your hand but can play all of them.  Other players are doing the same thing.  The more you play the game, the more you see during the game the possible strategies your opponents are pursuing and can effect the strategy they are going for.  It is never a good idea to let any player get particularly strong in any specific resource because you know they are likely going for an end game strategy that stems from that resource.  After winter tiles have been placed, players will bid in this season as usual.  Winter is very cutthroat and it is important to try to obtain start player going into winter and have a strong meeple advantage so that you can obtain the tile(s) you want.  

All in all, this is the best game ever designed.  The balance of strategy and tactics and the high level of control in the game lead to a satisfying experience where you feel like you have built a grand village and have exploited other neighboring villages to victory.  I haven't gotten into all of the aspects of the game (summer boats, start player acquisition, end of round boats, and more) but I can definitively state that all of the additional aspects that I haven't talked about only help to reinforce and enrich the incredible design that is Keyflower.

RATING: 7.5
The bidding for tiles, laying of tiles and their activation are the key mechanics and highlights of this board game. The random starting home tiles and tiles of each season, particularly spring, could lead to some imbalanced starting positions, e.g. a particular resource becoming extremely scarce. 

The game feels reasonably scalable. Downtime does not feel excessive, and may only be a slight issue with many players.

The icons do get in the way of the fine artwork of the game, which is a pity. The screens, whilst nicely drawn, are a little flimsy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Keyflower is a great eurogame about building the best settlements in a new land. It feels very different. I love the bidding system that uses workers, and building your village is also interesting, this is a winner for sure. I look forward to more plays.

My only point of criticism, early in my exploration of the game, is the random nature of the the seasonal tiles, and how they match up to the secret Winter tiles. It's rather difficult to stop someone from scoring a ton of bonus points during the Winter, while also trying to score points yourself. I'll have to see how that shakes out over more plays. Strangely enough, I think that the Farmers, and Merchants expansion, and the even larger variance which they provide, ease my fears. The game feels complete with those two expansions included.

RATING: 8.5
Keyflower does Euro stuff and it does it well.  The way meeples are used as unique currencies and activation pieces is really neat and drives a lot of the blocking and decision making in the game... but building your own little village and planning your moves to upgrade your tiles for points and better abilities is a lot of fun too.  

That being said I was surprised how thinky the game was and I would hate to play with a lot of AP prone players... and I found scoring to be more of a chore than I expected it would be (maybe its just hard with everyone around the table counting out loud at the same time! :laugh: ).  I might have to download the score sheet...  Regardless I'm looking forward to exploring this some more and could see my rating rising as the choices start feeling a little more second nature.

RATING: 7.9
Key Harvest managed to become January’s GotM. We wrote that it was the best game in the ‘Key’-series we played until then. Well, that was no lie, but it was also just the 2nd game we played in the series. But with the love for Keythedral and the even better experience with Key Harvest, we became very curious to Keyflower. Especially after receiving an email about the fact that this is a great game. After receiving that mail, we started looking for a nice deal on the game. Just a couple of weeks later, we found an second-handed copy of the game, that was only played once, for half the price it is new. We knew the seller, and we trust him blind. Made the deal and one day later, we are reading the rules of the game. I remember that the rule-set of the other two games in this series were very difficult to read. I believed that was because of the poor translation to Dutch. Now we had the original English version of the game. And indeed, the rules weren’t too hard to understand. This one seemed really like fun. Started playing the game, and the game just flows very naturally, and turned out to be very simple to play. But there are some hard decisions to make during playing. The unique auction system is working even with two, what is great, because most of the times, auctions just don’t work with two players. Minor thing. When playing with just two players, a lot in the game can be calculated. But still, this is a great game, and the best game we’ve played in the ‘Key’-series to date.

RATING: 8
Such interesting and unusual mechanisms that underly the very accessible interface:  "Bid" some keyples here to use this action; "bid" some keyples there to try to acquire the action space, which can allow you to collect the other players'… keyples!  The influx of keyples slows to a trickle by the winter, the fourth and final season, so luring other players' keyples to your town is crucial to keeping your supply of actions high for next season!  Wads of replay value in this, due to some reversible tiles, and not using all tiles (except in a 6p game — yes, 6p).
